Rational Knowledge and the Principle of Causality
Author summarizes rational knowledge characteristics and causality principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the characteristics of rational knowledge (necessity and universality) and explain how the principle of causality functions as a rational principle in Islamic thought, using al-Jurjani's definition.

Core Idea
Rational knowledge is necessary and universal; causality is a rational principle, not sensory.

You have learned that the intellect organizes sensory data. But what makes rational knowledge distinct? This passage lists two core characteristics: necessity and universality. It then dives into the principle of causality, showing how the intellect's ability to trace causes and effects is a foundational rational principle. Al-Jurjani's definition will help you see why causality is more than a habit—it's a rational necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two characteristics of rational knowledge
In the Arabic, find the two advantages of rational knowledge over sensory: necessity (al-ḍarūra) and universality (al-kulliyya). Note that necessity means rational knowledge traces back to self-evident truths.
2
Understand the intellect's retention versus senses' transience
The passage contrasts sensory perception (e.g., the eye) which cannot retain images, with the intellect which stores them. This highlights the intellect's power of abstraction.
3
Extract al-Jurjani's definition of causality ('illiyya)
Find the Arabic: 'ما يتوقف عليه وجود الشيء، ويكون خارجاً مؤثراً فيه'. Break it down: essential dependency, external, effective.
4
Compare sababiyya and 'illiyya
The passage notes some scholars distinguish sabab (concomitant with effect, possibly via intermediaries) from 'illa (direct efficient cause), but modern usage treats them as synonyms. Observe the author's evidence from the Arabic Language Academy.
5
Connect causality to scientific truth
The passage ends with all rational principles, including causality, as the foundation of scientific truths. Reflect on why this matters for Islamic sciences (e.g., Kalam proofs, Usul al-Fiqh ta'lil).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثالث: الفصل الثاني - العقل
خصائص العقل
في ضوء ما تقدم بوسعنا تلخيص خصائص المعرفة العقلية بما يلي:
(١ القدرة المبهرة على التنظيم والتصنيف، بعد جهل وعدم دراية، وذلك عن طريق تفاعله مع الحواس.
(۲) القدرة المبهرة على الاحتفاظ بصور الأشياء، بخلاف أي حاسة كالعين مثلا. فعلى الرغم من رؤيتها لشيء ما، وقدرتها على نقله إلى الذهن حسب قوانين الإبصار، إلا أنها عاجزة عن الاحتفاظ به على وجه التصريح، كما يفعل العقل حينما يختزن تلك الصور في حافظته العجيبة. فإن جئت لتبحث عن هذه الحافظة أو الخزان لا تجد شيئاً فأنى ذلك.
وأما ميزة المعرفة العقلية على الحسية فتتمثل في التالي: (١ الضرورة أي رجوع المعرفة إلى معلومات أولية ضرورية بحكم العقل، وإن لم تكن مكتسبة له بطريق النظر.
(۲) صدق التعميم أو الكلية: أي إنه نظراً لصدق وثبات المعرفة وشمولها لأفراد موضوعها فإنه بالإمكان تعميم الحكم على أفراد النوع كله على نحو يقيني وجازم.
صفة السببية (۳):
يتميز العقل بمقدرته على تعليل الظواهر وتفسيرها يارجاع كل سبب إلى مسببه، وكل نتيجة إلى مقدمتها، ونفي العشوائية والعبث عن الأحداث والمواقف. ونظراً لأهمية هذه السمة في حق العقل، فلا بأس من المضي في تفصيلها بما يثبت صلتها بالعقل وليس الحس كما قد يتوهم متوهم، ثم نتحدث عن موقعها في الفكر الإسلامي.
ويعرف الجرجاني العلية بقوله: ما يتوقف عليه وجود الشيء، ويكون خارجاً مؤثراً فيه. ومع أن تعريف العلية يساوي تعريف السببية من غير فرق حقيقي بينهما، إلا أن بعض الباحثين جعل السبب ما يحصل الشيء عنده أما العلة فتنشئ معلولها بلا واسطة بينهما ولا شرط، في حين أن السبب يفضي إلى الشيء بواسطة أو بوسائط. بيد أن اصطلاحات الباحثين - وخاصة في ميدان الدراسات الفلسفية - لم تلتفت إلى هذا التفريق، ودرجت على استخدام أي منهما للتعبير عن الآخر، ولعلّ في استخدام مجمع اللغة العربية ما يؤكد هذا المعنى، أعني عدم التفريق. فقد عرفها المعجم الفلسفي التابع للمجمع بأنها: (مبدأ عقلي يراد به أن لكل ظاهرة سبباً يحدثها). وعرف أحد الباحثين مبدأ العلية فقال: (استحالة حدوث شيء دون سبب أو علة تفسر حدوثه). ورادف باحث آخر بين السببية والعلية صراحة في غير موطن من دراسته الخاصة بضوابط المعرفة. وتعد المبادئ العقلية جميعاً ـ بما فيها مبدأ السببية - الأساس الأول لجميع الحقائق العلمية حتى أن قيمة النظريات والنتائج العلمية في المجالات التجريبية متوقفة على مدى دقتها في تطبيق تلك المبادئ على مجموعة
Chapter Three: Section Two - The Intellect. Characteristics of the Intellect. In light of what has preceded, we can summarize the characteristics of rational knowledge as follows: (1) The amazing ability to organize and classify, after ignorance and lack of awareness, through its interaction with the senses. (2) The amazing ability to retain images of things, unlike any sense such as the eye. For despite its seeing something and its ability to transmit it to the mind according to the laws of vision, it is incapable of retaining it explicitly, as the intellect does when it stores those images in its wonderful memory. If you come to search for this memory or storage, you find nothing, so how [could that be]? As for the advantage of rational knowledge over sensory knowledge, it is represented in the following: (1) Necessity, i.e., the return of knowledge to primary necessary information by virtue of the intellect, even if it is not acquired by it through reflection. (2) The truth of generalization or universality: i.e., because of the truth and stability of knowledge and its inclusion of all individuals of its subject, it is possible to generalize the judgment to all individuals of the whole species in a certain and decisive manner. The attribute of causality (3): The intellect is distinguished by its ability to explain phenomena and interpret them by tracing every cause to its effect, and every result to its premise, and denying randomness and futility from events and situations. Due to the importance of this characteristic for the intellect, it is appropriate to proceed in detailing it in a way that proves its connection to the intellect and not to the senses, as someone might imagine, and then we will discuss its place in Islamic thought. Al-Jurjani defines 'illiyya (causality) as: 'That upon which the existence of a thing depends, and which is external and effective in it.' Although the definition of 'illiyya is equal to the definition of sababiyya with no real difference between them, some researchers have made 'sabab' that at which the thing occurs, while 'illa' produces its effect without an intermediary between them nor a condition, whereas 'sabab' leads to the thing through one or more intermediaries. However, the terminologies of researchers - especially in the field of philosophical studies - have not paid attention to this distinction, and they have routinely used either one to express the other. Perhaps the usage of the Arabic Language Academy confirms this meaning, namely the lack of distinction. For the Philosophical Dictionary affiliated with the Academy defined it as: 'A rational principle by which it is intended that every phenomenon has a cause that produces it.' And one researcher defined the principle of 'illiyya saying: 'The impossibility of occurrence of something without a cause or an 'illa that explains its occurrence.' And another researcher equated sababiyya and 'illiyya explicitly in more than one place in his study on the controls of knowledge. And all rational principles - including the principle of causality - are the first foundation for all scientific truths, such that the value of scientific theories and results in experimental fields depends on the extent of their accuracy in applying those principles to the set.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion from a contemporary academic work, summarizing the characteristics of rational knowledge and introducing the principle of causality. The author first lists two key attributes of the intellect: its capacity for organization and classification after initial ignorance, and its remarkable ability to retain images of things, unlike the senses which cannot hold onto what they perceive. Then, the advantages of rational knowledge over sensory knowledge are presented: necessity (the intellect's reliance on self-evident primary truths) and the truth of generalization (the ability to arrive at universal judgments with certainty). The passage then focuses on the attribute of causality (sababiyya), defining it as the intellect's power to explain phenomena by tracing effects to their causes and results to their premises, thereby eliminating randomness. The author notes that al-Jurjani defines 'illiyya (causality) as that upon which existence depends, being external and effective. While some scholars distinguish between sabab (a cause that is concomitant with the effect but may involve intermediaries) and 'illa (a direct efficient cause), the philosophical tradition often uses the terms interchangeably, as confirmed by the Arabic Language Academy's dictionary. The passage concludes that all rational principles, including causality, form the foundational basis of scientific truths; the value of scientific theories and results rests on how precisely these principles are applied.

Key Terms
المعرفة العقلية
السببية
العلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between the principle of causality and scientific theories?
A) Scientific theories are independent of the principle of causality.
B) The value of scientific theories depends on how accurately they apply rational principles, including causality.
C) Causality is only relevant to philosophy, not science.
D) The principle of causality is derived from sensory experience alone.

Key Takeaway
Rational knowledge is characterized by necessity and universality, and the principle of causality is a rational, not sensory, foundation for all scientific knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the two characteristics of rational knowledge (necessity and universality) in the passage and explain why each is crucial for establishing certainty in Islamic theology (e.g., in proving the attributes of God).
?
Apply al-Jurjani's definition of 'illiyya to the ruling that wine is forbidden (ḥarām) because it intoxicates (iskār). Does the definition (dependency, external, effective) hold for the causal relationship between intoxication and prohibition? Write a brief analysis.

Defining Causality: The ‘illa and Sabab Distinction
Defines causality and discusses terminological distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the terms ‘illa and sabab as used in Islamic philosophy and identify the author's stance on their interchangeability.

Core Idea
Causality is a rational principle, not a mere empirical generalization.

In this passage, the author begins by citing al-Jurjani's definition of 'illiyya and then discusses a subtle distinction some scholars make between 'illa (efficient cause) and sabab (cause with intermediaries). However, he notes that in practice, philosophers use them interchangeably. Understanding this terminological nuance is crucial for reading classical and contemporary Islamic works on causality.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of 'illiyya
Find al-Jurjani's definition in the Arabic. What are its two key components?
The definition includes dependence and effectiveness.
2
Identify the proposed distinction
Some researchers distinguish between 'illa and sabab. What difference do they claim?
The text says 'illa produces directly, sabab through intermediaries.
3
Assess the author's conclusion
Does the author accept this distinction? What evidence does he provide for his stance?
He notes that philosophical usage blurs the distinction and cites the Arabic Language Academy.
4
Apply to a real example
Consider a standard example: fire burning cotton. Which term would be more appropriate according to each definition?
This helps internalize the difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويعرف الجرجاني العلية بقوله: ما يتوقف عليه وجود الشيء، ويكون خارجاً مؤثراً فيه. ومع أن تعريف العلية يساوي تعريف السببية من غير فرق حقيقي بينهما، إلا أن بعض الباحثين جعل السبب ما يحصل الشيء عنده أما العلة فتنشئ معلولها بلا واسطة بينهما ولا شرط، في حين أن السبب يفضي إلى الشيء بواسطة أو بوسائط. بيد أن اصطلاحات الباحثين - وخاصة في ميدان الدراسات الفلسفية - لم تلتفت إلى هذا التفريق، ودرجت على استخدام أي منهما للتعبير عن الآخر، ولعلّ في استخدام مجمع اللغة العربية ما يؤكد هذا المعنى، أعني عدم التفريق. فقد عرفها المعجم الفلسفي التابع للمجمع بأنها: (مبدأ عقلي يراد به أن لكل ظاهرة سبباً يحدثها). وعرف أحد الباحثين مبدأ العلية فقال: (استحالة حدوث شيء دون سبب أو علة تفسر حدوثه). ورادف باحث آخر بين السببية والعلية صراحة في غير موطن من دراسته الخاصة بضوابط المعرفة. وتعد المبادئ العقلية جميعاً ـ بما فيها مبدأ السببية - الأساس الأول لجميع الحقائق العلمية حتى أن قيمة النظريات والنتائج العلمية في المجالات التجريبية متوقفة على مدى دقتها في تطبيق تلك المبادئ على مجموعة التجارب التي أمكن الحصول عليها. وهذا يعني أن التجربة الحسية يعوزها تطبيق هذه المبادئ، ولا تنفك ملازمة لها، ومن غير المتصوّر إمكان استقلال الحس وحده من غير استناد إليها. فالتجربة عقلية - إذن - لقيامها على قوانين العقل الأولية. وفي ذلك تأكيد على أن الاختبار القبلي من شأن العقل، أما الاختبار البعدي فيرجع إلى الحس، بما يومئ إلى ضرورة تضافرهما معاً. وإذا كان مرد الخلاف بين وجهتي النظر سواء القائلة بانتماء السببية إلى العقل أو تلك القائلة بانتمائه إلى الحس إنما هو النزاع بين المذهبين العقلي والحسي، فإن النزاع في الفكر الإسلامي اتخذ منحى آخر. إذ تصدرت فرقة الأشاعرة لتبني اتجاه نفي السببية، وزعمت عدم وجود ضرورة في العلاقة بين العلة والمعلول، أو السبب والمسبب، وعدّت ما قد يظهر من تلازم بينهما مجرد اقتران بين شيئين، أو اطراد بين حادثتين، دون أن يكون لأحدهما أثر على الأخرى، بل الأمر مجرد تجاور عادي. إن هذا التفسير شبيه بتفسير الاتجاه التجريبي الذي تبلور بعد ذلك في الغرب، وتبناه أمثال لوك ومالبرانش وهيوم وبركلي، ثم تابعهم أغلب الوضعيين وفي مقدمتهم المؤسس الأول: أوجست كونت الذي رفض الميتافيزيقا لأنها غير خاضعة للتجربة، والاشتغال بها ـ عنده ـ يمثل لغواً، لقد شبهها (كونت) بألعاب الحواة أي التهريج. كما سفه (هيوم) فكرة السببية من أساسها.
Al-Jurjani defines causation (al-‘illiyya) by his saying: 'That upon which the existence of a thing depends, and which is external and effective in it.' Although the definition of ‘illiyya is equivalent to the definition of causality (al-sababiyya) without a real difference between them, some researchers have made al-sabab that by which a thing comes to be, while al-‘illa produces its effect with no intermediary between them and no condition, whereas al-sabab leads to the thing through an intermediary or intermediaries. However, the terminologies of researchers—especially in the field of philosophical studies—have not heeded this distinction, and they have come to use either to express the other; perhaps the usage of the Arabic Language Academy confirms this meaning, namely the lack of distinction. For the philosophical dictionary of the academy defined it as: 'A rational principle meant to indicate that every phenomenon has a cause that produces it.' And one researcher defined the principle of causation saying: 'The impossibility of something occurring without a cause or explanation (or cause) that explains its occurrence.' Another researcher explicitly equated causality and cause-effect relationship in more than one place in his study on the controls of knowledge. All rational principles—including the principle of causality—are considered the primary foundation for all scientific truths, such that the value of scientific theories and results in empirical fields depends on the extent of their accuracy in applying those principles to the set of experiments that can be obtained. This means that sensory experience lacks the application of these principles, and they are inseparable from it, and it is inconceivable for the sense alone to be independent without relying on them. Experience is therefore rational, because it is based on primary rational laws. In this is a confirmation that a priori testing is the business of reason, while a posteriori testing goes back to sense, indicating the necessity of their cooperation. And if the point of disagreement between the two viewpoints—whether saying that causality belongs to reason or that it belongs to sense—is the conflict between the rationalist and empiricist schools, then in Islamic thought the conflict took another path. The Ash'ari sect emerged to adopt a direction of denying causality, claiming that there is no necessity in the relation between cause and effect, or between the cause and the caused, and they considered what may appear as a correlation between them merely a conjunction of two things, or a regularity of two events, without either having an effect on the other; rather the matter is just a habitual conjunction. Indeed, this interpretation resembles the interpretation of the empirical trend that later crystallized in the West, adopted by the likes of Locke, Malebranche, Hume, and Berkeley, then followed by most positivists, foremost among them the first founder: Auguste Comte, who rejected metaphysics because it is not subject to experience, and occupying oneself with it—in his view—is nonsense; he likened it to the games of charlatans, i.e., buffoonery. And Hume disparaged the idea of causality from its very foundation.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion of the principle of causality (al-‘illiyya or al-sababiyya) and the debate over its nature and source. The author opens by citing al-Jurjani's definition: causality is that upon which a thing's existence depends, external and effective. Then the author notes a technical distinction made by some between 'cause' (sabab) and 'efficient cause' (‘illa), where ‘illa directly produces its effect without intermediary, while sabab leads to it through intermediaries. However, he observes that in practice philosophers use them interchangeably, and the Arabic Language Academy confirms this by defining causality as a rational principle stating that every phenomenon has a cause. The author then asserts that all rational principles, including causality, are the foundation of scientific truths; empirical experiments are only valid through applying these principles. Therefore, sensory experience is never independent of rational principles—experience is inherently rational because it relies on primary rational laws. A priori testing belongs to reason, a posteriori to sense, and both must cooperate. The author then contrasts the rationalist and empiricist dispute about whether causality belongs to reason or sense with the unique Islamic debate. The Ash'ari school denied causality, claiming no necessary relation between cause and effect—only habitual conjunction. This, the author notes, resembles the later Western empiricist trend (Locke, Malebranche, Hume, Berkeley) and positivism (Comte), who rejected metaphysics and, in Hume's case, undermined causality fundamentally. The passage thus presents a concise overview of the nature and history of the causality debate, emphasizing its rational foundation and critiquing the denial of necessary connection.

السببية
العقل

According to the passage, what is the position of most philosophical researchers on the distinction between 'illiyya and sababiyya?
A) They maintain a strict distinction.
B) They use them interchangeably.
C) They reject the term sababiyya entirely.
D) They only use 'illiyya.

Key Takeaway
The author, following common philosophical usage, treats 'illiyya and sababiyya as synonyms, emphasizing that causality is a rational principle necessary for all knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Causality as a Rational Principle: The Debate Over Its Origin
Discusses causality as a rational principle and the Ash'ari denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author considers causality a rational principle rather than an empirical generalization, and contrast the rationalist, empiricist, and Ash'ari positions.

Core Idea
Sensory experience alone cannot establish causation; reason is needed to infer necessity.

The author argues that all scientific knowledge rests on rational principles, including causality. He insists that empirical experiments are meaningless without these a priori principles. He then contrasts the rationalist view (causality belongs to reason) with the empiricist (it comes from sense), and points to the unique Islamic debate where the Ash'aris denied necessary causation, resembling later Western skepticism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's thesis on rational principles
What does he say about the role of rational principles in science? They are the foundation.
2
Explain why sensory experience is insufficient
Why does he say experience is rational? Because it relies on rational laws.
3
Contrast the rationalist and empiricist views
The dispute is whether causality belongs to reason or sense.
4
Analyze the Ash'ari position
What do they claim? How is it similar to Western empiricism?

ويعرف الجرجاني العلية بقوله: ما يتوقف عليه وجود الشيء، ويكون خارجاً مؤثراً فيه. ومع أن تعريف العلية يساوي تعريف السببية من غير فرق حقيقي بينهما، إلا أن بعض الباحثين جعل السبب ما يحصل الشيء عنده أما العلة فتنشئ معلولها بلا واسطة بينهما ولا شرط، في حين أن السبب يفضي إلى الشيء بواسطة أو بوسائط. بيد أن اصطلاحات الباحثين - وخاصة في ميدان الدراسات الفلسفية - لم تلتفت إلى هذا التفريق، ودرجت على استخدام أي منهما للتعبير عن الآخر، ولعلّ في استخدام مجمع اللغة العربية ما يؤكد هذا المعنى، أعني عدم التفريق. فقد عرفها المعجم الفلسفي التابع للمجمع بأنها: (مبدأ عقلي يراد به أن لكل ظاهرة سبباً يحدثها). وعرف أحد الباحثين مبدأ العلية فقال: (استحالة حدوث شيء دون سبب أو علة تفسر حدوثه). ورادف باحث آخر بين السببية والعلية صراحة في غير موطن من دراسته الخاصة بضوابط المعرفة. وتعد المبادئ العقلية جميعاً ـ بما فيها مبدأ السببية - الأساس الأول لجميع الحقائق العلمية حتى أن قيمة النظريات والنتائج العلمية في المجالات التجريبية متوقفة على مدى دقتها في تطبيق تلك المبادئ على مجموعة التجارب التي أمكن الحصول عليها. وهذا يعني أن التجربة الحسية يعوزها تطبيق هذه المبادئ، ولا تنفك ملازمة لها، ومن غير المتصوّر إمكان استقلال الحس وحده من غير استناد إليها. فالتجربة عقلية - إذن - لقيامها على قوانين العقل الأولية. وفي ذلك تأكيد على أن الاختبار القبلي من شأن العقل، أما الاختبار البعدي فيرجع إلى الحس، بما يومئ إلى ضرورة تضافرهما معاً. وإذا كان مرد الخلاف بين وجهتي النظر سواء القائلة بانتماء السببية إلى العقل أو تلك القائلة بانتمائه إلى الحس إنما هو النزاع بين المذهبين العقلي والحسي، فإن النزاع في الفكر الإسلامي اتخذ منحى آخر. إذ تصدرت فرقة الأشاعرة لتبني اتجاه نفي السببية، وزعمت عدم وجود ضرورة في العلاقة بين العلة والمعلول، أو السبب والمسبب، وعدّت ما قد يظهر من تلازم بينهما مجرد اقتران بين شيئين، أو اطراد بين حادثتين، دون أن يكون لأحدهما أثر على الأخرى، بل الأمر مجرد تجاور عادي. إن هذا التفسير شبيه بتفسير الاتجاه التجريبي الذي تبلور بعد ذلك في الغرب، وتبناه أمثال لوك ومالبرانش وهيوم وبركلي، ثم تابعهم أغلب الوضعيين وفي مقدمتهم المؤسس الأول: أوجست كونت الذي رفض الميتافيزيقا لأنها غير خاضعة للتجربة، والاشتغال بها ـ عنده ـ يمثل لغواً، لقد شبهها (كونت) بألعاب الحواة أي التهريج. كما سفه (هيوم) فكرة السببية من أساسها.
Al-Jurjani defines causation (al-‘illiyya) by his saying: 'That upon which the existence of a thing depends, and which is external and effective in it.' Although the definition of ‘illiyya is equivalent to the definition of causality (al-sababiyya) without a real difference between them, some researchers have made al-sabab that by which a thing comes to be, while al-‘illa produces its effect with no intermediary between them and no condition, whereas al-sabab leads to the thing through an intermediary or intermediaries. However, the terminologies of researchers—especially in the field of philosophical studies—have not heeded this distinction, and they have come to use either to express the other; perhaps the usage of the Arabic Language Academy confirms this meaning, namely the lack of distinction. For the philosophical dictionary of the academy defined it as: 'A rational principle meant to indicate that every phenomenon has a cause that produces it.' And one researcher defined the principle of causation saying: 'The impossibility of something occurring without a cause or explanation (or cause) that explains its occurrence.' Another researcher explicitly equated causality and cause-effect relationship in more than one place in his study on the controls of knowledge. All rational principles—including the principle of causality—are considered the primary foundation for all scientific truths, such that the value of scientific theories and results in empirical fields depends on the extent of their accuracy in applying those principles to the set of experiments that can be obtained. This means that sensory experience lacks the application of these principles, and they are inseparable from it, and it is inconceivable for the sense alone to be independent without relying on them. Experience is therefore rational, because it is based on primary rational laws. In this is a confirmation that a priori testing is the business of reason, while a posteriori testing goes back to sense, indicating the necessity of their cooperation. And if the point of disagreement between the two viewpoints—whether saying that causality belongs to reason or that it belongs to sense—is the conflict between the rationalist and empiricist schools, then in Islamic thought the conflict took another path. The Ash'ari sect emerged to adopt a direction of denying causality, claiming that there is no necessity in the relation between cause and effect, or between the cause and the caused, and they considered what may appear as a correlation between them merely a conjunction of two things, or a regularity of two events, without either having an effect on the other; rather the matter is just a habitual conjunction. Indeed, this interpretation resembles the interpretation of the empirical trend that later crystallized in the West, adopted by the likes of Locke, Malebranche, Hume, and Berkeley, then followed by most positivists, foremost among them the first founder: Auguste Comte, who rejected metaphysics because it is not subject to experience, and occupying oneself with it—in his view—is nonsense; he likened it to the games of charlatans, i.e., buffoonery. And Hume disparaged the idea of causality from its very foundation.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion of the principle of causality (al-‘illiyya or al-sababiyya) and the debate over its nature and source. The author opens by citing al-Jurjani's definition: causality is that upon which a thing's existence depends, external and effective. Then the author notes a technical distinction made by some between 'cause' (sabab) and 'efficient cause' (‘illa), where ‘illa directly produces its effect without intermediary, while sabab leads to it through intermediaries. However, he observes that in practice philosophers use them interchangeably, and the Arabic Language Academy confirms this by defining causality as a rational principle stating that every phenomenon has a cause. The author then asserts that all rational principles, including causality, are the foundation of scientific truths; empirical experiments are only valid through applying these principles. Therefore, sensory experience is never independent of rational principles—experience is inherently rational because it relies on primary rational laws. A priori testing belongs to reason, a posteriori to sense, and both must cooperate. The author then contrasts the rationalist and empiricist dispute about whether causality belongs to reason or sense with the unique Islamic debate. The Ash'ari school denied causality, claiming no necessary relation between cause and effect—only habitual conjunction. This, the author notes, resembles the later Western empiricist trend (Locke, Malebranche, Hume, Berkeley) and positivism (Comte), who rejected metaphysics and, in Hume's case, undermined causality fundamentally. The passage thus presents a concise overview of the nature and history of the causality debate, emphasizing its rational foundation and critiquing the denial of necessary connection.

Key Terms
العلية
السببية
العقل
الحس
الأشاعرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) They are independent.
B) They must cooperate.
C) A priori is superior.
D) A posteriori is sufficient.

Key Takeaway
The author maintains causality is an a priori rational principle, denying that sense experience alone can establish it, and critiques the Ash'ari/Humean denial of necessary connection.

Ghazali's Critique of Necessary Causality
Ghazali argues that the connection between cause and effect is not necessary but depends on God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ghazali's argument against necessary causality and identify his alternative explanation based on divine omnipotence.

Core Idea
Ghazali denies necessary causality; God can create effects without typical causes.

In this passage from Tahafut al-Falasifa, Ghazali launches a devastating critique of the philosophers' claim that causes necessarily produce their effects. He argues that the observed regularities are simply God's custom, not inherent necessities. This argument is foundational to the Ash'ari occasionalist worldview and remains influential in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ghazali's target
Locate the doctrine that Ghazali is attacking: the philosophers' claim that causes necessarily produce their effects. He calls this 'the necessity of causality' and counts it among their falsehoods.
The target is the necessary connection, not causality per se.
2
Extract Ghazali's examples
List the pairs of events Ghazali gives as examples of customary conjunctions: drinking and quenching thirst, eating and satiety, fire and burning, sunlight and light, cutting the neck and death, etc. Note that these are all common observations.
These examples are chosen to illustrate that the conjunction is not logically necessary.
3
Analyze his logical move
Ghazali argues that because the two things are distinct and not logically entailed by each other (affirmation/negation of one does not imply the other), their conjunction is not necessary. The link is only due to God's prior decree.
This is a logical argument from the distinctness of the relata.
4
Understand his alternative
Ghazali attributes the regular conjunction to God's creative will and habit (al-'ada). It is within God's power to create the effect without the cause or the cause without the effect. This establishes occasionalism.
The true agent is God alone; secondary causes are merely occasions.
5
Assess the implications for miracles
Since God is not bound by His habit, miracles (khariq al-'ada) are possible. For example, fire could touch cotton without burning, or cotton could burn without fire. This defends the possibility of prophetic miracles.
This refutes the philosophers who denied miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لقد عد الغزالي القول بضرورة السببية من أباطيل الفلاسفة. ونراه يفند المتصلة بهذا المبدأ فيقول في (تهافت الفلاسفة): المقولة السابعة عشرة : في إبطال قولهم في استحالة خرق العادات الاقتران بين ما يعتقد في العادة سبباً وبين ما يعتقد مسبباً ليس ضرورياً عندنا، بل كل شيئين ليس هذا ذاك، ولا ذاك هذا، ولا إثبات أحدهما متضمناً لإثبات الآخر، ولا نفيه متضمناً لنفي الآخر فليس من ضرورة وجود أحدهما وجود الآخر، ولا من ضرورة عدم أحدهما عدم الآخر، مثل الري والشرب والشبع و الأكل، والاحتراق ولقاء النار، والنور وطلوع الشمس والموت وجز الرقبة والشفاء وشرب الدواء، وإسهال البطن واستعمال المسهل، وهلم جراً إلى كل المشاهدات من المقترنات في الطب والنجوم والصناعات والحرف فإن اقترانها لما سبق من تقدير الله سبحانه – بخلقها على التساوق لا لكونه ضرورياً في نفسه، غير قابل للفرق، بل في المقدور خلق الشبع دون الأكل، وخلق الموت دون جز الرقبة، وإدامة الحياة مع جز الرقبة وهلم جراً إلى المقترنات. وأنكر الفلاسفة إمكانه وادعوا استحالة. - والنظر في هذه الأمور الخارجة عن الحصر يطول فلنعين مثالاً واحداً وهو الاحتراق في القطن عند ملاقاة النار، فإننا نجوّز وقوع الملاقاة بينهما دون الاحتراق، ونجوّز حدوث انقلاب القطن رماداً محترقاً دون ملاقاة النار، وهم ينكرون جوازه)). إن الغزالي إذ ينكر مبدأ السببية أو العلية فإنه يحيل هذه الأسباب ومسبباتها جميعاً إلى الله - تعالى - ليجعله الفاعل الحقيقي لها.
Al-Ghazali counted the statement of the necessity of causality among the falsehoods of the philosophers. And we see him refuting what is connected to this principle, saying in his 'Tahafut al-Falasifa': The seventeenth discussion: On invalidating their claim about the impossibility of breaking habits. The conjunction between what is customarily believed to be a cause and what is believed to be an effect is not necessary according to us. Rather, any two things where this is not that, and that is not this, and the affirmation of one does not entail the affirmation of the other, nor does the negation of one entail the negation of the other, then it is not necessary that the existence of one involves the existence of the other, nor that the non-existence of one involves the non-existence of the other, such as drinking and quenching thirst, eating and satiety, burning and contact with fire, light and sunrise, death and severing of the neck, recovery and drinking medicine, loosening of the bowels and use of a laxative, and so on to all observable conjunctions in medicine, astronomy, crafts, and trades. For their conjunction is due to a prior decree of God – by His creating them in succession – not because it is necessary in itself, not admitting of separation. Rather, it is within [divine] power to create satiety without eating, and to create death without severing the neck, and to sustain life along with severing the neck, and so on to all conjunctions. The philosophers denied this possibility and claimed its impossibility. - Consideration of these countless matters would be lengthy, so let us specify one example: the burning of cotton upon contact with fire. For we allow the occurrence of contact between them without burning, and we allow the occurrence of the cotton turning into burnt ashes without contact with fire, while they deny its possibility. Al-Ghazali, since he denies the principle of causality or efficient causality, refers all these causes and their effects to God – exalted – to make Him the true agent of them.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ghazali's famous refutation of the necessary connection between causes and effects. He begins by stating that he considers the claim of causality's necessity as one of the philosophers' falsehoods. Then he quotes from his work 'Tahafut al-Falasifa' the 17th discussion: On the impossibility of breaking habits. He argues that the conjunction between what is customarily considered cause and effect is not necessary. He gives examples: drinking and quenching thirst, eating and satiety, fire and burning, sunlight and light, cutting the neck and death, etc. He says that it is possible for God to create the effect without the cause, or the cause without the effect, as He wills. The philosophers deny this possibility. Then the commentator explains that Ghazali thus rejects the principle of causality and attributes all actions to God alone.

Key Terms
السببية (al-sababiyya): The necessary connection between cause and effect, which Ghazali denies.
العادة (al-'ada): The regular pattern of events that God typically creates, but which can be broken by miracles.
خارق العادة (khariq al-'ada): An event that breaks the customary pattern, possible by God's power.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does Ghazali claim is possible regarding fire and cotton?
A) Fire necessarily burns cotton.
B) Fire touches cotton without burning it.
C) Cotton cannot be burned.
D) Cotton burns without fire.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's critique of necessary causality is a cornerstone of Ash'ari occasionalism, establishing that all events are directly caused by God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct Ghazali's argument using one of his examples (e.g., fire and cotton) in a premise-conclusion format.
?
Choose a reported miracle from Islamic tradition (e.g., the splitting of the moon, the well of Zamzam) and explain how Ghazali's critique accounts for it without violating the principle of causality.

Reconciling Causality with Tawhid
Critique of Ash'ari occasionalism and a reconciliatory view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key objection to the Ash'ari position on causality and explain the reconciliatory view that preserves both causality and divine omnipotence.

Core Idea
Causality as a natural system does not conflict with God's absolute power.

In this passage, the author critiques the Ash'ari assumption that attributing effects to secondary causes leads to shirk. He argues that this fear is unfounded because causality as a natural system does not diminish God's power. He then presents a reconciliatory view: God creates both causes and effects, and can break the 'habit' (al-'ada) through miracles. Understanding this debate is crucial for relating natural laws to tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ash'ari assumption
Find where the author states the Ash'ari (and Ghazali) assumption about causality leading to shirk. What exactly do they fear?
The assumption is that attributing effect to secondary causes implies believing in two true agents, which is shirk.
2
Understand the author's objection
The author says this assumption is incorrect. Why? Locate the phrase 'لا يصطدم بقدرة الله ومشيئته المطلقة' and explain how causality as a natural system can coexist with divine power.
Causality is a system created by God; it does not compete with His power.
3
Explore the reconciliatory view
The author mentions a reconciliatory attempt. What is the core affirmation of that view? Look for 'جميع هذه الأسباب المركبة في الأشياء مخلوقة لخالق واحد'.
All composite causes are created by God; their working does not negate His power to stop them.
4
Clarify al-Ghazali's actual position
The passage claims Ghazali did not deny causality absolutely. What did he actually oppose? Find where it says he warned against a certain kind of causality.
He opposed subjective causality that denies the first cause or God's ability to break the habit.
5
Apply to Islamic theology
Consider a miracle like the splitting of the moon. How would the reconciliatory view explain it without denying physical causation?
Miracles are God breaking the habit (al-'ada) while preserving the normal causal system at other times.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحق أن أهم مأخذ يمكن أن يواجه به الأشاعرة – ومعهم الغزالي – يتمثل في افتراضهم أن القول بإسناد التأثير لتلك الأسباب ينتهي بنا إلى الشرك، لأن القول بمبدأ السببية يلزم منه القول بفاعلين حقيقيين، وذلك ليس صحيحاً، إذ القول بالسببية من حيث هي نظام طبيعي لا يصطدم بقدرة الله ومشيئته المطلقة، ويصبح - تبعاً لهذا - التسليم بأن الله خالق الأسباب ومسبباتها بدهيــة مباشرة (1) . وكأن بعض الباحثين أدرك حقيقة الافتراق الذي أدى إليه الخلاف حول مسألة العلية هذه من حيث نزاع الباحثين العملي واسع الهوة فيها فسعى - في محاولة توفيقية - إلى أن يخفف من غلواء الجدل الذي دار بين الغزالي ومن يرى رأيه في نفي السببية مطلقاً - وإن كان ظاهر الأمر كذلك ـ أو اعتماد ابن رشد ومن يرى رأيه السببية فاعلاً كلياً - ولو لم يكن ثمة ما يقنع بذلك. إن الموقف الذي يجلي الغموض، ولعله أن يقرّب بين وجهات النظر حول الموضوع يكمن في تقرير أن جميع هذه الأسباب المركبة في الأشياء مخلوقة لخالق واحد هو الله - سبحانه وتعالى. وإن عملها في مسبباتها لا يعني انتفاء قدرة الموجد الأول الأوحد لها على إيقاف عملها، بل إنه سبحانه – شاء إخراج الأمر من (العادة) إلى (خارق العادة لحكمة يريدها الله- سبحانه -. والغزالي لم يصنع أكثر من الحذر والتحذير من أن يكون الحديث عن علاقة الضرورة بين الأسباب والمسببات مضيفاً إلى القول بالسببية الذاتية التي تنكر السبب الأول، أو تنكر إمكان وجواز تدخله لنقض ((العادة))، وإيقاف عمل الأسباب في المسببات بواسطة اسباب أخرى غير عادية يوجدها الله - سبحانه وتعالى -. وذلك يعني إيمانه بالسبية ولكنه ينبه فقط على سببية لا يدركها ولا يسلّم بها الذين لا يؤمنون.
And the truth is that the most important objection that can be made against the Ash'aris – and with them al-Ghazali – consists in their assumption that the statement of attributing effect to those leads to polytheism. Because the statement of the principle of causality necessitates the statement of two true agents, and that is not correct. For the statement of causality as a natural system does not conflict with God's absolute power and will, and consequently the admission that God is the creator of causes and their effects becomes a direct self-evident truth. It is as if some researchers realized the reality of the divergence that the disagreement over the issue of causality led to, in terms of the wide practical gap in the researchers' dispute, so they sought – in a reconciliatory attempt – to reduce the intensity of the debate that took place between al-Ghazali and those who share his view in denying causality absolutely – even if the apparent matter is so – or adopting Ibn Rushd and those who share his view of causality as a universal agent – even if there is nothing to convince that. The position that removes ambiguity, and perhaps it brings closer the points of view on the subject, lies in affirming that all these composite causes in things are created by one creator, who is God – glorified and exalted. And their working in their effects does not mean the negation of the power of the first, sole originator to stop their working. Rather, He – glorified – willed to bring the matter from (habit) to (breaking the habit) for a wisdom that God – glorified – wants. And al-Ghazali did nothing more than caution and warn against speaking about the relationship of necessity between causes and effects, adding to the statement of subjective causality that denies the first cause, or denies the possibility and permissibility of His intervention to break the (habit), and stop the working of causes in their effects by means of other extraordinary causes that God – glorified and exalted – creates. That means his belief in causality, but he only alerts to a causality that those who do not believe do not perceive nor accept.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage presents a critique of the Ash'ari position (including Ghazali) regarding causality. The author states that the most important objection that can be made against them is their assumption that attributing effect to causes leads to shirk (polytheism). He argues that this is incorrect, because saying that causality is a natural system does not conflict with God's absolute power and will. Rather, it is self-evident that God is the creator of both causes and effects. Then the author mentions that some researchers attempted a reconciliatory view: all the composite causes in things are created by God, and their working does not negate God's power to stop them. God may break the habit (custom) for a wisdom. Ghazali only warned against speaking of necessary connection between causes and effects that would deny the first cause or its intervention. Thus Ghazali actually believed in causality, but only a causality that does not deny God.

Key Terms
السببية (al-sababiyya)
الشرك (al-shirk)
العادة (al-'ada)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the reconciliatory view, what does Ghazali's position actually imply about causality?
A) He denies any causality.
B) He accepts causality but warns against denying God's power to break it.
C) He believes causality is necessary.
D) He rejects both causality and God's power.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari claim that causality leads to shirk is refuted by recognizing that causality as a created system does not compete with God's power; Ghazali himself accepted a form of causality while cautioning against necessary connection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Take a miracle from the Quran (e.g., the splitting of the moon) and explain, using the reconciliatory view, how it can be understood without denying usual physical causation.

The Quranic Epistemology of Causality: Regularity and Miracles
Author presents Quranic epistemology of causality, affirming natural laws and miracles as divine creations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Quranic view of causality as a divinely created system of regularities that can be suspended for miracles, and distinguish it from both occasionalism and naturalism.

Core Idea
Causality is a divinely created regularity, not a necessity; God can suspend it for miracles.

In this passage, the author navigates the historical debate between Ibn Rushd and al-Ghazali on causality, presenting a Quran-based resolution that affirms both natural laws and divine intervention. This lesson unpacks that resolution, showing how the Quranic sunna allows for consistent cause-effect relationships while reserving God's power to interrupt them. You will learn to identify the key elements of this epistemology and apply it to understand how miracles fit within a lawful universe.

Guided Path
1
Locate the conciliatory point
The text begins by noting that Ibn Rushd and al-Ghazali agree that created beings are not self-sufficient but require an external agent. Identify this agreement and note its significance for the whole discussion.
The agreement sets the stage for a Quranic resolution that avoids extremes.
2
Isolate the criticized position
The author dismisses certain questions as meaningless. Which questions? Why does he consider them unproductive? Look at the examples of fire burning and knife cutting.
The author believes these questions distract from the real issue: understanding God's sunna.
3
Extract the definition of sunna
The author gives examples of sunna: fire burns, food satiates, medicine heals, seed germinates. How does he describe these? Note that they are 'the making of God' and 'unchanging.'
Sunna here means consistent divine patterns, not independent natural laws.
4
Reconcile sunna with miracles
The author states that God can break the usual consistency of sunna for a miracle or warning. Find the exact phrase and explain how this preserves both regularity and divine power.
The phrase is 'when the will of the Creator wishes to break this usual consistency.'
5
Apply to Quranic evidence
The passage ends with the first revealed verses. How does Surah al-Alaq demonstrate the unity of causality? Man is created from a clot—a lowly cause. This shows all existence is subject to causality by divine decree.
The clot symbolizes that even the noblest creation arises from a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وابن رشد لا يختلف في حقيقة الأمر مع الغزالي إذ ينص على أن فعل الموجودات ليس من بعضها في بعض، أو أنها مكتفية بأنفسها، بل يقر ابن رشد بأن ثمة فاعلاً خارجياً فعله شرط في فعلها ووجودها. وإذا كان هذا رأياً توفيقياً لا يسلّم له بإطلاق فإن هناك ما هو أكثر إثارة للنقاش في هذا السياق، وذلك هو الاتجاه الداعي إلى طي صفحة ذلك الخلاف فما عاد هناك ما يسوغ استدعاءه اليوم. فأسئلة من مثل هل النار تحرق أم أن الله - تعالى - هو الذي يحدث فيها الحرق؟ وهل السكين هي التي تقطع أم أن الله يحدث القطع فيها عند الجز ... إلخ؟ لا معنى لها، بل الذي يعنينا هو إدراك سنة الله تعالى أن جعل المادة القابلة للاحتراق تحترق حين تقع في النار، وأن يخلق الشبع عند تناول الطعام والشفاء مع الدواء، والإنبات عند توافر الشروط للبذرة. فهذه الصفات الخاصة بالمادة سواء صفة الذرة أو صفة مركباتها جميعها من صنع الله - تعالى - ومخلوقاته ذات السنن التي لا تتغير ولا تتبدل. والمنهج القرآني يعترف بفعل السنن والقوانين في المسببات المتولّدة عنها، ولكنه يؤكد في الوقت ذاته على أن هذه السنن أو القوانين شأنها شأن الظواهر الأخرى من حيث إنها جميعاً مخلوقة لله - تعالى - فهي وإن كانت تسير وفق نظام مطّرد ومحكم لكن ذلك لن ينسينا في أي لحظة أنها جزء من خلق الله، أو جزء من إرادته، حيث إذا شاءت إرادة الخالق المبدع خرق هذا الاطراد المعتاد لعمل هذه السنن، لإظهار معجزة الرسول، أو تحذير لأقوام أو أفراد لا يعترفون لله - تعالى - بالألوهية والربوبية، فإن ذلك القانون يتوقف أو يخرق لهذا الغرض مثلاً. وفي ضوء هذا تبرز وحدة السببية على النحو الذي يلخصه أحد الباحثين بقوله: فوحدة السببية تبدو في جميع الموجودات - بما فيها الإنسان - خاضعة في نشوئها واستحالتها لعلل وأسباب، هي عوامل النشوء والاستحالة، وإن تكن عوامل فعلها ليس من ذاتها، بل من القدرة الإلهية. ولعل أول آيات القرآن نزولاً تشير إلى هذا المعنى، إذ يقول تعالى: اقْرَأْ بِاسْمِ رَبِّكَ الَّذِي خَلَقَ، خَلَقَ الإِنْسانَ مِنْ عَلَقٍ [العلق: ١/٩٦-٢]. فالإنسان وهو أشرف المخلوقات ليس إلا مخلوقاً من سبب وهو العلق، وهو من أتفه الموجودات، بحيث تخضع كلها لقانون السببية بالتقدير الإلهي.
And Ibn Rushd does not differ in reality with al-Ghazali, as he states that the action of beings is not from one another, nor are they self-sufficient. Rather, Ibn Rushd affirms that there is an external agent whose action is a condition for their action and existence. And if this is a conciliatory view that is not absolutely accepted, there is something more provocative for discussion in this context, namely the trend calling for turning the page of that dispute, as there is no longer anything justifying its recall today. Questions such as: Does fire burn, or is it God Almighty who produces burning in it? Does the knife cut, or does God produce cutting in it when it contacts ... etc.? They are meaningless. Rather, what concerns us is understanding the sunna of God Almighty that He made flammable matter burn when it falls into fire, and that He creates satiety upon eating food, recovery with medicine, and germination when conditions are met for the seed. These properties specific to matter, whether the property of the atom or the property of its compounds, are all the making of God Almighty and His creations with unchanging and unalterable sunan. The Quranic methodology recognizes the action of sunan and laws in the effects generated from them, but at the same time it emphasizes that these sunan or laws are like other phenomena in that they are all created by God Almighty. So even though they proceed according to a consistent and precise system, that will not make us forget at any moment that they are part of God's creation or part of His will. For when the will of the Creator, the Originator, wishes to break this usual consistency in the operation of these sunan, to manifest a miracle of the Prophet, or to warn people or individuals who do not acknowledge God Almighty's divinity and lordship, then that law stops or is broken for that purpose, for example. In light of this, the unity of causality emerges in the manner summarized by one researcher in his saying: The unity of causality appears in all existents - including man - subject in their origination and transformation to causes and reasons, which are factors of origination and transformation, even though the factors of their action are not from themselves but from divine power. Perhaps the first verses of the Quran to be revealed indicate this meaning, as He says: 'Read in the name of your Lord who created, created man from a clot' (Surah al-Alaq 96:1-2). Man, who is the noblest of creatures, is nothing but a creature from a cause, which is the clot, one of the most insignificant of existents, such that all are subject to the law of causality by divine decree.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage presents the Quranic epistemological stance on causality, aiming to reconcile the regularity of natural laws with the possibility of miracles. The author begins by noting the agreement between Ibn Rushd (Averroes) and al-Ghazali on a key point: the actions of created beings are not self-sufficient but require an external agent whose action is a condition for their existence and operation. This is a conciliatory view, but the author immediately criticizes a more extreme trend that wants to dismiss the entire historical debate as meaningless. The author argues that questions like 'Does fire burn or does God create burning?' are not the real issue. Instead, the focus should be on understanding the divine sunna: God has established a consistent system where certain causes are regularly followed by certain effects (fire causes burning, food causes satiety, medicine causes recovery, etc.). These cause-effect relationships are themselves created by God and constitute unchanging laws. However, the Quranic methodology, while affirming the action of these laws, simultaneously asserts that they are created and subject to God's will. Therefore, they can be interrupted for divine purposes such as miracles or warnings. The author then cites a researcher's summary: all existents are subject to causality, but the effective factors are from divine power, not from themselves. Finally, the author points to the first revealed verses (Surah al-Alaq) as evidence: man, the noblest creation, is created from a lowly cause (the clot), showing that all things, including man, are subject to the law of causality by divine decree. The overall message is that causality is a divinely ordained system that operates consistently but can be suspended for specific purposes, preserving both scientific regularity and the possibility of miracles.

Key Terms
سببية (sababiōyya): In this passage, sababōyya refers to the principle of cause and effect as a created, regular system ordained by God, not as an independent metaphysical necessity. [Note: This term is debated in kalām; Ashʿarīs typically deny necessary causal connections, while this text affirms them as divinely created regularities.]
سنة (sunna): Sunna here means the divinely established, consistent pattern or law governing natural phenomena, such as burning, satiety, or germination. [Note: Contrast with sharʿī usage of sunna as recommended practice; here it is a cosmological/legal term for natural law.]
معجزة (muʿjiza): A miracle, defined as a supernatural event that breaks the usual consistency of the divinely ordained sunna, serving to validate prophethood. [Note: In classical mantiq, muʿjiza is discussed in the context of proof of prophethood, but here it is used to illustrate the flexibility of causal laws.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Quranic view on the relationship between natural causes and miracles?
A) Natural causes operate independently, and miracles are impossible.
B) Natural causes are illusions; only God acts directly.
C) Natural causes are created regularities that can be interrupted by God for miracles.
D) Miracles are actually natural events misunderstood by humans.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic epistemology of causality affirms both the consistent operation of natural laws as divine creations and the possibility of their suspension for miracles, preserving God's absolute sovereignty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the following statement: 'Medicine cures because God creates health when medicine is taken, but He can cure without medicine as a miracle.' Identify which part reflects the sunna and which part reflects divine will. Does this statement align with the passage's position?
?
Read Q. 36:82: 'His command, when He intends a thing, is only to say to it, "Be," and it is.' Explain how this verse supports the idea that causality is created and subject to divine will, as presented in the passage.

Critique of Superficial Engagement with the Causality Debate
Author critiques a modern writer's misuse of the causality debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical confusion in a modern writer's use of 'scientific thinking' regarding causality.

Core Idea
Scientific thinking is not a single thing; the empiricists who founded it denied the very causality the writer wants to teach.

In this passage, the author confronts a contemporary Muslim educational writer who presents the historical debate between Ibn Rushd and al-Ghazali on causality as one of four great crises. The writer calls for modern education to adopt 'scientific logic' based on 'scientific causality'. The author shows that this call is based on a misunderstanding of what 'scientific thinking' actually means. This lesson will help you see the logical trap: the writer's own preferred educational philosophy contradicts his demand.

Guided Path
1
Identify the writer's claim
The writer claims that the historical dispute over causality is a crisis and that contemporary education should settle in favor of 'scientific logic' represented by 'scientific causality'. Pinpoint this claim in the Arabic.
Look for 'حسم الموقف لصالح المنطق العلمي' and 'السببية العلمية'.
2
Extract the author's key question
The author asks: 'What does the writer mean by scientific thinking? Does he mean the empirical method or the rational method?' This question is the pivot of the critique.
The Arabic: 'ماذا يقصد الكاتب بالتفكير العلمي؟ هل يعني المنهج التجريبي ؟ أم المنهج العقلي؟'
3
Analyze the empirical horn
If the writer means empirical method, the author points out that the founders of empiricism (Locke, Hume, etc.) denied causality or necessary connection. So the writer cannot simply adopt empiricism without accepting its implications.
The author lists 'لوك ومالبرانش وهيوم وبركلي'.
4
Analyze the rational horn
If the writer means rational method, then his call for 'contemporary education' contradicts itself because the dominant education system (pragmatism) is rooted in empiricism, not rationalism.
The text says: 'التربية البراجماتية... من منابع هذه المدرسة الاتجاه التجريبي الوضعي'.
5
Conclude the logical contradiction
The writer's argument is self-undermining: both possible interpretations of 'scientific thinking' lead to a conflict with his call for scientific causality in education. The author exposes this inconsistency.
This shows the writer lacks conceptual clarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنك لتعجب غاية العجب حين يفاجئك كاتب تربوي مسلم يعد ذلك الاختلاف التاريخي حول السببية واحداً من أزمات أربع كبيرة في نظره. فتراه يعد ذلك اضطراباً في تفكيرنا حين نقف حيارى أمام السببية في الفكر النقلي. ويقارن بين ابن رشد - كممثل للعقلانيين - وبين الغزالي - كممثل للنقليين - فهذا الأخير يرفض مطلقاً مبدأ السببية لأن القول بذلك سيؤدي إلى إنكار المعجزة، وهي أساس النبوات. وهنا يقرر الكاتب أن التربية المعاصرة - يقصد في البلدان الإسلامية - عجزت عن حسم الموقف لصالح المنطق العلمي، ممثلاً بالسببية العلمية، وتربية الناشئة عليها. والسؤال المنطقي هنا : ماذا يقصد الكاتب بالتفكير العلمي؟ هل يعني المنهج التجريبي ؟ أم المنهج العقلي؟ فإن قصد الأول فليراجع مايقوله رواد ذلك الاتجاه في نفي السببية أمثال لوك ومالبرانش وهيوم وبركلي، فهل يعني ذلك ميتافيزيقيتهم العلوية أيضاً ؟ أما إن قصد الآخر (العقلي) فإنه سيتناقض مع دعوته التربية المعاصرة في البلدان الإسلامية إلى حسم الموقف لصالح المنطق العلمي، إذ من المعلوم أن التربية السائدة اليوم في الغرب كما هي – ولو نظرياً - في معظم بلاد المسلمين التربية (البراجماتية التي يطلق عليها التربية الحديثة أو المعاصرة، ومن منابع هذه المدرسة الاتجاه التجريبي الوضعي.
And you would be extremely amazed when a Muslim educational writer surprises you by considering that historical difference over causality to be one of four great crises in his view. You see him counting it as a disturbance in our thinking when we stand confused before causality in transmitted thought. He compares Ibn Rushd - as a representative of rationalists - and al-Ghazali - as a representative of traditionalists - and the latter absolutely rejects the principle of causality because saying it would lead to denial of miracles, which is the basis of prophecies. Here the writer declares that contemporary education - meaning in Islamic countries - has failed to resolve the stance in favor of scientific logic, represented by scientific causality, and raising the younger generation upon it. The logical question here: What does the writer mean by scientific thinking? Does he mean the empirical method? Or the rational method? If he means the first, let him review what the pioneers of that trend say regarding the denial of causality, such as Locke, Malebranche, Hume, and Berkeley. Does that mean their transcendent metaphysics too? But if he means the other (the rational), then he will contradict his call for contemporary education in Islamic countries to settle the stance in favor of scientific logic, since it is known that the prevailing education today in the West, as it is - even theoretically - in most Muslim countries, is pragmatic education, which is called modern or contemporary education, and among the sources of this school is the empirical positivist trend.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage critically engages with a contemporary Muslim educational writer who presents the historical debate on causality (particularly between Ibn Rushd and al-Ghazali) as one of the four great crises facing Islamic thought. The author first expresses astonishment at this claim. The writer is described as viewing the debate as a source of confusion in 'transmitted thought' (al-fikr al-naqlī), and he contrasts Ibn Rushd (representing rationalists) and al-Ghazali (traditionalists), asserting that al-Ghazali totally rejects causality because it would negate miracles. The writer then concludes that modern education in Islamic countries has failed to adopt 'scientific logic' based on scientific causality. The author then poses a logical question: what does the writer mean by 'scientific thinking'? Is it empirical or rational? If empirical, the author points out that empiricist philosophers like Locke, Malebranche, Hume, and Berkeley actually denied causality (or at least necessary connection), so the writer cannot simply adopt empiricism without accepting its metaphysical implications. If rational, then the writer contradicts himself because the dominant educational philosophy in the West (and in many Muslim countries) is pragmatism, which is rooted in empiricism and positivism, not rationalism. The author thus exposes an inconsistency: the writer's call for modern education based on scientific causality is undermined by the fact that the very scientific tradition he invokes (empiricism) tends to deny causality or treat it as a mental habit, while the pragmatic education he likely endorses is itself rooted in that empiricist tradition. The passage is a critique of superficial engagement with philosophical issues and a call for conceptual clarity.

Key Terms
سببية علمية
منطق علمي
تربية معاصرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What logical contradiction does the author identify in the educational writer's argument?
A) The writer claims al-Ghazali rejected causality, but he actually accepted it.
B) If the writer means empirical method, its founders denied causality; if rational, his call contradicts the prevailing pragmatic education rooted in empiricism.
C) Ibn Rushd and al-Ghazali actually agreed on causality, so the writer is wrong.
D) The writer ignores that miracles are impossible in a scientific worldview.

Key Takeaway
The author exposes a logical inconsistency: the modern writer's call for scientific causality in education is undermined by the fact that the empirical tradition he appeals to actually denies causality.

1. Identify a contemporary Muslim writer's argument about science and Islam (you may use the one from this passage). Then apply the author's dilemma: ask whether the writer means empirical or rational method, and check if the writer's own educational stance is consistent.

2. Read a short excerpt from a modern Islamic education textbook that advocates for 'scientific thinking'. Using the critique method from this lesson, analyze whether the term is used unambiguously and whether the author's broader commitments align.

The Middle Position on Generated Effects
Author introduces generated effects and the middle position on causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of al-mutawalladāt and how the author's middle position avoids dualism between divine and human causality.

Core Idea
Causality is not dualism: effects arise from both human action and divine causation.

The author introduces a crucial concept in Islamic theology: how can we affirm causality without falling into dualism? The answer lies in the notion of 'generated effects' (al-mutawalladāt). Let's break down the opening argument to understand this middle position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term 'generated effects'
Find the Arabic term al-mutawalladāt in the passage. Notice that the author defines them as things occurring due to both the servant's action and God's created causes.
This term is key to understanding the compatibilist framework.
2
Analyze the example of satiety
The author gives the example of satiety (shib‘). It results from the servant's eating and swallowing, but also from the powers God placed in the human body and in food. This shows two contributory factors.
3
Explain why this avoids dualism
The author argues that this does not imply a rivalry or parity between Creator and creature. The premise harmonizes with God's absolute efficacy and His command to follow natural laws.
Dualism would mean treating human action as independent of God, which is avoided.
4
Summarize the middle position
Concisely state: causality is affirmed, but it is not a zero-sum game; divine and human causality coexist without conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثالث: الفصل الثاني - العقل
الوسط أن هذه الأمور التي يقال لها المتولّدات حاصلة بسبب فعل العبد، وبالأسباب الأخرى التي يخلقها الله. فالشبع يحصل بأكل العبد وابتلاعه، وبما جعله الله في الإنسان، وفي الغذاء من القوى المعينة على حصول الشبع (١). إذن فإن القول بالسببية أمر يؤكده الإيمان أو الوحي من غير نشوب مفتعل لثنائية الخالق أو الفاعل وقدرة المخلوق وفاعليته على نحو من الندية والمنازعة. ذلك أن المسلّمة الأساس هنا تنسجم مع خالقية الله – تعالى – وفاعليته المطلقة، وأمره - سبحانه - لنا بالسير وفق منطق السنن المبثوثة في عالمي الآفاق والأنفس، وفي ضوء قانون السير والنظر:
وَقُلْ سِيرُوا فِي الْأَرْضِ فَانظُرُوا كَيْفَ بَدَأَ الْخَلْق ﴾ [العنكبوت: ٢٠/٢٩]. وهذه الآية تشير بوضوح إلى أن بداية الخلق ليست خاصة بعالم الأنفس فقط، كما قد يتبادر إلى الذهن السطحي، بل إن السير في الأرض يتحقق بتتبع صنع الله وآياته في الخلق والإنشاء، في الجمادات والكائنات الحية على السواء (٣). وفي ذلك ما يشير إلى أن لا تعارض بين حركة العبد النسبية وقدرة الله المطلقة. ولكون الإنسان أرقى مخلوق لاستخلافه دون سواه، فإن الخطاب القرآني يشير الله الجامعة بين قدرة الله المطلقة وحركة العبد النسبية المحدودة. ولكن لا بد أن يكون ذلك وفق قراءة كلية تجمع بين الآيات في مواضعها المختلفة، حتى لا نقع في النظرة التجزيئية للنص القرآني. فإذا كان الله تعالى قد قال:
وَنُرِيدُ أَن لَمُنَّ عَلَى الَّذِينَ اسْتُضْعِفُوا فِي الْأَرْضِ وَنَجْعَلَهُمْ أَئِمَّةً وَنَجْعَلَهُمُ الْوَارِثِينَ، وَتُمَكِّنَ لَهُمْ فِي الأَرْضِ وَنُرِيَ فِرْعَوْنَ وَهَامَانَ وَجُنُودَهُما مِنْهُمْ ما كانُوا يَحْذَرُونَ﴾ [القصص: ٥/٢٨-٦].
وإنَّ الْأَرْضَ لِلَّهِ يُورِثُهَا مَنْ يَشَاءُ مِنْ عِبَادِهِ وَالْعَاقِبَةُ لِلْمُتَّقِينَ ﴾ [الأعراف: ١٢٨/٧].
الباب الثالث: الفصل الثاني - العقل
وَإِذا أَرَدْنا أَنْ نُهْلِكَ قَرْيَةً أَمَرْنَا مُتْرَفِيها فَفَسَقُوا فِيهَا فَحَقَّ عَلَيْهَا الْقَوْلُ فَدَمَّرْناها تدميرا (١) [الإسراء: ١٦/١٧].
فلا بد أن تفهم تلك الآيات في ضوء ما تقرره آيات أخرى تصرح بمسؤولية الإنسان:
وَكُلَّ إنسان أَلْزَمْنَاهُ طَائِرَهُ فِي عُنُقِهِ... [الإسراء: ١٣/١٧].
وَأَن لَيْسَ لِلإِنسان إلا ما سعى... [النجم: ٣٩/٥٣].
فَمَنْ يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْراً يَرَهُ، وَمَنْ يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ شَرًا يَرَهُ [الزلزلة: ٧/٩٩-٨].
و نفهمها كذلك في ضوء تلك الآيات المقررة لحرية الإنسان واختياره وإرادته:
وَهَدَيْنَاهُ النَّجْدَيْنِ﴾ [البلد: ١٠/٩٠].
وإنا هَدَيْناهُ السَّبِيلَ إمّا شاكِراً وَإِما كَفُوراً) [الإنسان: ٣/٧٦].
وإلى جانب تأكيد الوحي على حتمية منطق السننية ممثلة في مظهر السببية وقانونها معا ؛ فإن منطق العلم هو الآخر يرفض فكرة التلقائية والمصادفة والظروف الطارئة، بل يعتمد على قانون السبب والمسبب (السببية). وذلك ما يعبر عنه منهج البحث العلمي اليوم بمصطلح المتغيرين المستقل (٢) والتابع (٣). ولذلك فالحتمية السببية مفهوم أساسي يقوم عليه العلم، وفي ضوئها يتم تفسير الظواهر المختلفة. وذلك ما ينبغي أن تتبناه وتشدد على التزامه التربية الإسلامية، وأن تعكس ذلك عبر عناصر المنهج. وأن يعزز ذلك قيام المعلم بتفسير الظواهر المختلفة أثناء تدريسه وفق قانون السببية، كي يتحقق الأمر ذاته في المتعلم.
Chapter Three, Section Two: The Intellect
The middle [position] is that these things called generated effects occur due to the action of the servant and due to the other causes that God creates. For satiety occurs by the servant's eating and swallowing, and by what God has placed in humans and in food of powers that assist in achieving satiety (1). Therefore, the statement of causality is a matter affirmed by faith or revelation, without an artificial emergence of a duality of the Creator or the agent and the creature's ability and efficacy in a manner of parity and contention. That is because the basic premise here harmonizes with God's creatorship and His absolute efficacy, and His command for us to proceed according to the logic of the laws spread in the worlds of horizons and souls, and in light of the law of travel and contemplation:
"And say, 'Travel throughout the earth and see how He began creation'" [al-Ankabut 20/29]. This verse clearly indicates that the beginning of creation is not specific to the world of souls only, as might come to a superficial mind, but rather travel in the earth is achieved by tracing God's making and His signs in creation and origination, in inanimate objects and living beings alike (3). In that is an indication that there is no contradiction between the relative movement of the servant and God's absolute power. And because man is the most noble creature due to his vicegerency uniquely, the Quranic discourse indicates the comprehensive [relationship] between God's absolute power and the relative limited movement of the servant. But this must be according to a holistic reading that brings together the verses in their different places, so that we do not fall into a fragmentary view of the Quranic text. For if Allah has said:
"And We intended to confer favor upon those who were oppressed in the land and make them leaders and make them the inheritors, and establish them in the land and show Pharaoh and Haman and their soldiers that which they feared" [al-Qasas 5/28-6].
"And indeed, the earth belongs to Allah; He causes to inherit it whom He wills of His servants, and the [best] outcome is for the righteous" [al-A'raf 128/7].
Chapter Three, Section Two: The Intellect
"And when We intend to destroy a city, We command its affluent, and they commit wickedness therein, so the word is justified against it, and We destroy it utterly" [al-Isra 16/17].
So it is necessary to understand those verses in light of what other verses establish that explicitly state human responsibility:
"And every human being We have bound his fate to his neck..." [al-Isra 13/17].
"And that man has only what he strives for..." [al-Najm 39/53].
"So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it, and whoever does an atom's weight of evil will see it" [al-Zalzala 7/99-8].
And we understand them also in light of those verses that establish human freedom, choice, and will:
"And We guided him to the two paths" [al-Balad 10/90].
"Indeed, We guided him to the way, either grateful or ungrateful" [al-Insan 3/76].
And alongside revelation's affirmation of the inevitability of the logic of the laws (sunan) represented in the appearance of causality and its law together, the logic of science also rejects the idea of spontaneity, coincidence, and emergent circumstances, but rather relies on the law of cause and effect (causality). That is what is expressed today by scientific research methodology in the term independent variable (2) and dependent variable (3). Therefore, causal determinism is a fundamental concept upon which science is built, and in its light various phenomena are interpreted. That is what Islamic education should adopt and emphasize its commitment, and reflect that through the elements of the curriculum. And that should be reinforced by the teacher explaining various phenomena during his teaching according to the law of causality, so that the same thing is realized in the learner.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological argument for the law of causality (sababiyya) from both revelation and science. The author begins by stating the middle position (al-wasat) regarding generated effects (al-mutawalladāt): they occur due to the action of the servant (fi‘l al-‘abd) and due to other causes created by God. The example of satiety (shib‘) is given: it results from the servant's eating and swallowing, but also from the powers God placed in the human body and in food. This shows that causality does not imply a dualistic rivalry between Creator and creature; rather, it is affirmed by faith and revelation without such a conflict. The author then quotes the Quranic command to travel and see how creation began, interpreting it as an invitation to observe causality in both the physical world and the human soul. This verse, he argues, refutes a superficial understanding that limits creation to the inner world—it includes inanimate objects and living beings alike. The passage then emphasizes that there is no contradiction between the relative movement of the servant and God's absolute power. The Quranic discourse, particularly verses on divine will and human responsibility, must be read holistically to avoid a fragmented view. Verses such as 'And We intended to confer favor upon those who were oppressed' and 'Indeed, the earth belongs to Allah' are cited alongside verses affirming human responsibility and free will: 'Every human being We have bound his fate to his neck,' 'Man has only what he strives for,' and the atom's weight of good and evil. These are reconciled with verses on free will: 'We guided him to the two paths.' The author then turns to science, noting that alongside revelation's affirmation of the law of causality, the logic of science also rejects spontaneity and coincidence, relying on cause and effect. Modern scientific methodology expresses this with the terms independent variable (al-mutaghayyir al-mustaqill) and dependent variable (al-tābi‘). Thus, causal determinism (al-ḥatmiyya al-sababiyya) is a foundational concept of science, and Islamic education should adopt and emphasize it, with teachers explaining phenomena through the lens of causality so that students internalize this principle.

Key Terms
المتولّدات
السببية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, satiety results from:
A) The servant's eating alone.
B) God's direct creation without any human action.
C) Both the servant's eating and the powers God placed in the human and food.
D) Random chance.

Key Takeaway
The author's middle position harmonizes human causality with divine omnipotence, avoiding both occasionalism and dualism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the act of writing a book. Describe it in terms of generated effects: identify the human action (author's writing) and the divine causes involved (the existence of pen, paper, ink, the author's ability and health). Explain how this does not imply dualism.

Quranic Evidence for Causality: A Holistic Reading
Author presents Quranic evidence for causality and urges holistic reading.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to argue from the Quran that causality is affirmed and that verses on divine will and human responsibility must be read together holistically.

Core Idea
The Quran commands us to observe causality in nature while also affirming human responsibility.

The author quotes several Quranic verses to ground causality in revelation. But careful: one must not take verses in isolation. Let's see how he reconciles verses on divine control with those on human freedom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the verses about travel and observation
Find the verse 'Travel throughout the earth and see how He began creation' (29:20). The author interprets this as an invitation to observe causality in both the physical world and the human soul.
The verse is not limited to spiritual reflection; it includes studying natural phenomena.
2
Note verses affirming divine power over history
The author cites verses like 'And We intended to confer favor upon those who were oppressed...' (28:5-6) and 'Indeed, the earth belongs to Allah...' (7:128). These show that ultimate outcomes are in God's hands.
3
Contrast with verses on human responsibility and free will
The author lists verses: 'Every human being We have bound his fate to his neck' (17:13), 'Man has only what he strives for' (53:39), and the atom's weight of good and evil (99:7-8). Also, 'We guided him to the two paths' (90:10) and 'We guided him to the way, either grateful or ungrateful' (76:3).
These emphasize human accountability and choice.
4
Understand the principle of holistic reading
The author insists on a holistic reading (qirā'a kulliyya) that brings together all these verses. One must not take verses in isolation, otherwise one may fall into a fragmentary view that pits divine will against human freedom.
A holistic approach reveals that causality and human responsibility are compatible.
