وزبدة القول: إن القرآن لايرى تعارضاً بيناً بين مهام الحس ومهام العقل، بل على العكس من ذلك يرى التفاعل الإيجابي بينهما، والتكامل والتعاضد. أما القول بغير ذلك فتوهّم مزعوم عند التحقيق ذلك أن المعرفة العقلية اليقينية لا تتحقق تماماً، ولا تستحق هذا الوصف إلا حين يبني العقل أحكامه مستنداً على واحد من ثلاثة: فإما اعتماداً على أحد قوانين العقل الأولية الفطرية القطعية، وإما اعتماداً على ما يفيد اليقين من شهادات الحس، أو استناداً إلى الشهادات الخبرية القطعية، وهذا المستند الثالث سنتناوله بالتفصيل فيما سيأتي من هذه الدراسة - يإذن الله تعالى -
(۱) محمد علي الجوزو، مفهوم العقل والقلب في القرآن والسنة، ۲۰۸.
Third Chapter: Second Section - The Intellect. That is because they loved the worldly life over the Hereafter and that Allah does not guide the disbelieving people. Those are the ones upon whose hearts, hearing, and sight Allah has set a seal, and those are the heedless. [Surah an-Nahl 16:107-108]. And We have placed over their hearts covers lest they understand it, and in their ears deafness. And when you mention your Lord alone in the Qur'an, they turn back in aversion. We are most knowing of what they listen to when they listen to you and when they are in private conversation, when the wrongdoers say, 'You follow not but a man possessed.' [Surah al-Isra 17:46-47]. Aaman. O you who have believed, obey Allah and His Messenger and do not turn from him while you hear [his command]. And do not be like those who say, 'We have heard,' while they do not hear. Indeed, the worst of living creatures in the sight of Allah are the deaf and dumb who do not use reason. And if Allah had known any good in them, He would have made them hear. And if He had made them hear, they would have turned away while they were refusing. [Surah al-Anfal 8:20-23]. And we find the Qur'an likening those who do not utilize their sensory and intellectual tools of knowledge to cattle, based on the commonality of not actually benefiting from them. Rather, man in this state is more astray than cattle, as they are submissive to Allah, the Exalted, like other non-accountable creatures: 'Or do you think that most of them hear or reason? They are not except like cattle. Rather, they are more astray in way.' [Surah al-Furqan 25:44]. And We have certainly created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind. They have hearts with which they do not understand, they have eyes with which they do not see, and they have ears with which they do not hear. Those are like cattle; rather, they are more astray. Those are the heedless. [Surah al-A'raf 7:179]. Attention is drawn in these verses to the connection linking the senses to the heart, as if all of them (hearing, sight, and heart) operate simultaneously. This connection, from another angle, affirms the connection of the intellect to the heart, and the connection of both together to the senses. They are either all closed, or all open to truth, light, and guidance. Finally, the disbelievers' disabling of their sensory and intellectual tools in this world necessitates that these tools receive a share of punishment in the Hereafter: 'And We turn their hearts and their sights away, just as they did not believe in it the first time, and We leave them in their transgression, wandering blindly.' [Surah al-An'am 6:110]. And they will say, 'If only we had listened or used our reason, we would not be among the companions of the Blaze.' [Surah al-Mulk 67:10]. And We will gather them on the Day of Resurrection on their faces, blind, dumb, and deaf. [Surah al-Isra 17:97]. The fire of Allah, kindled, which rises over the hearts. [Surah al-Humazah 104:6-7]. And concerning the center of thinking in the human brain, which is the forelock, He said: 'No! If he does not desist, We will surely seize [him] by the forelock, a lying, sinning forelock.' [Surah al-Alaq 96:15-16]. The bottom line is that the Qur'an does not see a clear contradiction between the functions of the senses and the functions of the intellect; on the contrary, it sees positive interaction, integration, and synergy between them. As for saying otherwise, it is a false assumption upon investigation, because certain rational knowledge is not fully realized nor does it deserve this description except when the intellect builds its judgments based on one of three: either relying on one of the primary, innate, certain laws of the intellect, or relying on what yields certainty from the testimonies of the senses, or relying on certain report-based testimonies. We will discuss this third foundation in detail later in this study, Allah willing. (1) Muhammad Ali al-Juzu, The Concept of the Intellect and Heart in the Qur'an and Sunnah, p. 208.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological analysis of the Quranic perspective on the relationship between sensory perception (al-hiss) and intellectual reasoning (al-'aql). The author begins by citing several Quranic verses that describe the sealing of hearts, hearing, and sight of disbelievers (Surah an-Nahl, al-Isra, al-Anfal) to illustrate that their failure to use these faculties leads to ignorance. He then notes that the Quran likens those who do not utilize their sensory and intellectual tools to cattle (Surah al-Furqan, al-A'raf), emphasizing that such people are even more astray because they are accountable. The author highlights that these verses show a connection between the senses and the heart (al-qalb), suggesting that they operate together: either all are closed to guidance or all are open. He further argues that the disabling of these tools in this world leads to punishment in the Hereafter, citing verses about the turning of hearts and sights on the Day of Judgment (Surah al-An'am, al-Mulk, al-Isra, al-Humazah, al-Alaq). The main conclusion is that the Quran sees no conflict between the senses and intellect; rather, they interact positively and complement each other. The author asserts that certain rational knowledge (ma'rifah 'aqliyyah yaqiniyyah) is only achieved when the intellect builds its judgments on one of three foundations: (1) innate certain laws of reason, (2) certain sensory testimony, or (3) certain transmitted reports (which will be discussed later). Thus, the passage defends the integration of sensory and rational faculties in Islamic epistemology.

الحس
القلب

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the relationship between the senses and the intellect as portrayed in the Quran?
A) The senses are unnecessary; only intellect matters.
B) There is a clear contradiction; intellect supersedes senses.
C) They are complementary and work together.
D) They are unrelated faculties.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verses show that senses and intellect are meant to work together, not in opposition, for the attainment of knowledge and guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Three Foundations of Certain Knowledge in Islamic Epistemology
Author outlines three foundations for certain rational knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the three sources of certain rational knowledge according to the author.

Core Idea
Certainty requires relying on innate reason, senses, or reliable reports.

At the end of the passage, the author presents a crucial epistemological principle: certain rational knowledge (al-ma'rifah al-'aqliyyah al-yaqiniyyah) is built on one of three foundations. This classification is important for understanding how Islamic scholars evaluate the certainty of knowledge claims in fields like usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Locate the tripartite classification
Read the final paragraph of the passage: from 'وزبدة القول' to the end. Identify the three sources listed.
The author says certain knowledge is based on one of three: innate rational laws, sensory testimony, or certain reports.
2
Define the first foundation: innate rational laws
These are primary, innate, certain principles of reason, such as the law of non-contradiction. They are self-evident and not derived from experience.
In usul al-fiqh, these are sometimes called 'al-awwaliyyat'.
3
Define the second foundation: sensory testimony
This refers to direct sensory perception that yields certainty, e.g., seeing the sun rise. The author emphasizes that sensory input, when reliable, can ground certain knowledge.
Classical scholars debate the certainty of senses but accept them under normal conditions.
4
Define the third foundation: certain reports
These are transmitted reports that reach the level of tawatur (mass transmission) or are otherwise deemed reliable. The author promises to discuss this in detail later.
This corresponds to khabar mutawatir in hadith terminology.
5
Reflect on the overall epistemology
Note that the author integrates all three: reason, senses, and transmitted knowledge. This tripartite classification emphasizes that Islamic epistemology does not rely solely on one source.
The author's position counters claims that Islam is anti-rational or anti-empirical.

يا أَيُّها الذِينَ آمَنُوا أَطِيعُوا اللَّهَ وَرَسُولَهُ وَلا تَوَلَّوْا عَنْهُ وَأَنْتُمْ تَسْمَعُونَ، وَلا تَكُونُوا كَالَّذِينَ قَالُوا سَمِعْنا وَهُمْ لا يَسْمَعُونَ، إِنَّ شَرَّ الدَّوابِّ عِندَ اللهِ الصُّمُّ الْبُكْمُ الَّذِينَ لا يَعْقِلُونَ، وَلَوْ عَلِمَ اللهُ فِيهِمْ خَيْراً لأَسْمَعَهُمْ وَلَوْ أَسْمَعَهُمْ لَتَوَلَّوْا وَهُمْ مُعْرِضُونَ ﴾ [الأنفال: ۲۰/۸ - ۲۳].
ونجد القرآن يشبه الذين لا يستغلون أدوات معرفتهم الحسية والعقلية بالأنعام بجامع عدم الإفادة الحقيقية منها، بل يكون الإنسان - والحال هذه – أضل من الأنعام إذ هي مستسلمة لله - تعالى - كسائر المخلوقات غير المكلفة: أَمْ تَحْسَبُ أَنَّ أَكْثَرَهُمْ يَسْمَعُونَ أَوْ يَعْقِلُونَ إِنْ هُمْ إِلَّا كَالأَنْعَامِ بَلْ هُمْ أَضَلُّ سَبِيلاً) [الفرقان: ٤٤/٢٥].

وزبدة القول: إن القرآن لايرى تعارضاً بيناً بين مهام الحس ومهام العقل، بل على العكس من ذلك يرى التفاعل الإيجابي بينهما، والتكامل والتعاضد. أما القول بغير ذلك فتوهّم مزعوم عند التحقيق ذلك أن المعرفة العقلية اليقينية لا تتحقق تماماً، ولا تستحق هذا الوصف إلا حين يبني العقل أحكامه مستنداً على واحد من ثلاثة: فإما اعتماداً على أحد قوانين العقل الأولية الفطرية القطعية، وإما اعتماداً على ما يفيد اليقين من شهادات الحس، أو استناداً إلى الشهادات الخبرية القطعية، وهذا المستند الثالث سنتناوله بالتفصيل فيما سيأتي من هذه الدراسة - يإذن الله تعالى -
(۱) محمد علي الجوزو، مفهوم العقل والقلب في القرآن والسنة، ۲۰۸.
Third Chapter: Second Section - The Intellect. That is because they loved the worldly life over the Hereafter and that Allah does not guide the disbelieving people. Those are the ones upon whose hearts, hearing, and sight Allah has set a seal, and those are the heedless. [Surah an-Nahl 16:107-108]. And We have placed over their hearts covers lest they understand it, and in their ears deafness. And when you mention your Lord alone in the Qur'an, they turn back in aversion. We are most knowing of what they listen to when they listen to you and when they are in private conversation, when the wrongdoers say, 'You follow not but a man possessed.' [Surah al-Isra 17:46-47]. Aaman. O you who have believed, obey Allah and His Messenger and do not turn from him while you hear [his command]. And do not be like those who say, 'We have heard,' while they do not hear. Indeed, the worst of living creatures in the sight of Allah are the deaf and dumb who do not use reason. And if Allah had known any good in them, He would have made them hear. And if He had made them hear, they would have turned away while they were refusing. [Surah al-Anfal 8:20-23]. And we find the Qur'an likening those who do not utilize their sensory and intellectual tools of knowledge to cattle, based on the commonality of not actually benefiting from them. Rather, man in this state is more astray than cattle, as they are submissive to Allah, the Exalted, like other non-accountable creatures: 'Or do you think that most of them hear or reason? They are not except like cattle. Rather, they are more astray in way.' [Surah al-Furqan 25:44]. And We have certainly created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind. They have hearts with which they do not understand, they have eyes with which they do not see, and they have ears with which they do not hear. Those are like cattle; rather, they are more astray. Those are the heedless. [Surah al-A'raf 7:179]. Attention is drawn in these verses to the connection linking the senses to the heart, as if all of them (hearing, sight, and heart) operate simultaneously. This connection, from another angle, affirms the connection of the intellect to the heart, and the connection of both together to the senses. They are either all closed, or all open to truth, light, and guidance. Finally, the disbelievers' disabling of their sensory and intellectual tools in this world necessitates that these tools receive a share of punishment in the Hereafter: 'And We turn their hearts and their sights away, just as they did not believe in it the first time, and We leave them in their transgression, wandering blindly.' [Surah al-An'am 6:110]. And they will say, 'If only we had listened or used our reason, we would not be among the companions of the Blaze.' [Surah al-Mulk 67:10]. And We will gather them on the Day of Resurrection on their faces, blind, dumb, and deaf. [Surah al-Isra 17:97]. The fire of Allah, kindled, which rises over the hearts. [Surah al-Humazah 104:6-7]. And concerning the center of thinking in the human brain, which is the forelock, He said: 'No! If he does not desist, We will surely seize [him] by the forelock, a lying, sinning forelock.' [Surah al-Alaq 96:15-16]. The bottom line is that the Qur'an does not see a clear contradiction between the functions of the senses and the functions of the intellect; on the contrary, it sees positive interaction, integration, and synergy between them. As for saying otherwise, it is a false assumption upon investigation, because certain rational knowledge is not fully realized nor does it deserve this description except when the intellect builds its judgments based on one of three: either relying on one of the primary, innate, certain laws of the intellect, or relying on what yields certainty from the testimonies of the senses, or relying on certain report-based testimonies. We will discuss this third foundation in detail later in this study, Allah willing. (1) Muhammad Ali al-Juzu, The Concept of the Intellect and Heart in the Qur'an and Sunnah, p. 208.

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological analysis of the Quranic perspective on the relationship between sensory perception (al-hiss) and intellectual reasoning (al-'aql). The author begins by citing several Quranic verses that describe the sealing of hearts, hearing, and sight of disbelievers (Surah an-Nahl, al-Isra, al-Anfal) to illustrate that their failure to use these faculties leads to ignorance. He then notes that the Quran likens those who do not utilize their sensory and intellectual tools to cattle (Surah al-Furqan, al-A'raf), emphasizing that such people are even more astray because they are accountable. The author highlights that these verses show a connection between the senses and the heart (al-qalb), suggesting that they operate together: either all are closed to guidance or all are open. He further argues that the disabling of these tools in this world leads to punishment in the Hereafter, citing verses about the turning of hearts and sights on the Day of Judgment (Surah al-An'am, al-Mulk, al-Isra, al-Humazah, al-Alaq). The main conclusion is that the Quran sees no conflict between the senses and intellect; rather, they interact positively and complement each other. The author asserts that certain rational knowledge (ma'rifah 'aqliyyah yaqiniyyah) is only achieved when the intellect builds its judgments on one of three foundations: (1) innate certain laws of reason, (2) certain sensory testimony, or (3) certain transmitted reports (which will be discussed later). Thus, the passage defends the integration of sensory and rational faculties in Islamic epistemology.

The Quranic Integration of Intellect, Heart, and Senses
Author argues Quran sees no conflict between sense and reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic perspective on the relationship between intellect, heart, and senses as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
The intellect, heart, and senses work together, not in opposition.

This lesson explores a key Quranic epistemological principle: the integration of intellect and senses. The passage argues that the Quran does not pit reason against sense perception but sees them as complementary faculties that, when properly used, lead to truth. The author supports this with Quranic verses showing that disbelief results from disabling these God-given tools.

Guided Path
1
Locate the key terms in the Arabic
Find words for intellect (العقل), heart (القلب), and senses (الحواس). Notice the phrase 'ارتباط العقل بالقلب، وارتباط الاثنين معاً بالحواس'. This establishes the connection.
The term 'qalb' in Quranic epistemology often denotes a cognitive and spiritual center.
2
Identify the relationship claimed
The author says these faculties are either 'all closed or all open to truth, light, and guidance'. This suggests they operate in unison, not independently.
3
Find the Quranic verses cited
Identify verses like 6:110, 67:10, 17:97, 104:6-7, and 96:15-16. These illustrate punishment for disabling sensory and intellectual faculties.
The verses show that failure to use intellect and senses leads to spiritual blindness and punishment.
4
Synthesize the Quranic view
Combine the verses and the author's claim: the Quran sees positive interaction, complementarity, and mutual support between senses and intellect, not conflict.
Any claim of contradiction is dismissed as a 'false illusion'.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يؤكد من جانب آخر ارتباط العقل بالقلب، وارتباط الاثنين معاً بالحواس، فهي إما مغلقة جميعاً، وإما مفتوحة جميعاً على الحق والنور والهداية.
وأخيراً فإن تعطيل الكفار لأدواتهم الحسية والعقلية في الدنيا يستوجب أن تنال هذه الأدوات قسطاً من العقاب في الآخرة:
وَنُقَلبُ أَفْئِدَتَهُمْ وَأَبْصارَهُمْ كَما لَمْ يُؤْمِنُوا بِهِ أَوَّلَ مَرَّةٍ وَنَذَرُهُمْ فِي طغيانِهِمْ يَعْمَهُونَ﴾ [الأنعام: ١١٠/٦].
وَ قالُوا لَوْ كُنَّا نَسْمَعُ أَوْ نَعْقِلُ ما كُنا فِي أَصحاب السعيركم ولك: ١٠/٦٧]. وَنَحْشُرُهُمْ يَوْمَ الْقِيَامَةِ عَلَى وُجُوهِهِمْ عُمْياً وبُكْماً وَصُمَام [الإسراء:١٩٧/١٧.
نارُ اللهِ الْمُوقَدَةُ الَّتِي تَطْلِعُ عَلَى الأَفْئِدَةِ) الهمزة: ٦/١٠٤ - ٧]. وعن مركز التفكير في مخ الإنسان وهي الناصية قال تعالى: ﴿كَلاً لَئِنْ لَمْ يَنتَهِ لَنَسْفَعاً بِالنَّاصِيَةِ، ناصِيَةٍ كاذبة خاطفة (العلق: ١٥/٩٦ - ١٦].
وزبدة القول: إن القرآن لايرى تعارضاً بيناً بين مهام الحس ومهام العقل، بل على العكس من ذلك يرى التفاعل الإيجابي بينهما، والتكامل والتعاضد. أما القول بغير ذلك فتوهّم مزعوم عند التحقيق ذلك أن المعرفة العقلية اليقينية لا تتحقق تماماً، ولا تستحق هذا الوصف إلا حين يبني العقل أحكامه مستنداً على واحد من ثلاثة: فإما اعتماداً على أحد قوانين العقل الأولية الفطرية القطعية، وإما اعتماداً على ما يفيد اليقين من شهادات الحس، أو استناداً إلى الشهادات الخبرية القطعية، وهذا المستند الثالث سنتناوله بالتفصيل فيما سيأتي من هذه الدراسة - يإذن الله تعالى -
وهنا يراعى ضرورة إحداث عملية موازنة بين المدركات. فالموازين الفطرية - وليس المكتسبة - أبعد الخطأ من شهادات الحس وشهادات الآخرين غير عن المعصومين.
العقل في هذه الحال المحك لفحص مدركات الحس ويصبح وتمحيص شهادات الآخرين، وأخبارهم عن المعارف.
وفي ضوء ذلك يقوم العقل بتصنيف المعارف القادمة إليه من الطريقين السابقين لوضع كل منهما في رتبته المناسبة يقيناً أو ترجيحاً، أو ما هو دون الرجحان. ثم الرفض حال حكمه باستحالة أو امتناع ما يدخل في نطاق قوانينه الذاتية. وعليه فليس كل ما ورد إلى الجهاز الفكري المدرك في درجة واحدة، بل أعلاها الحق بيقين وأدناها الباطل بيقين وبينهما الظن الراجح، والشك والظن المرجوح - كما سبق القول فيما مضى.
وبالنسبة إلى علاقة ذلك بالعملية التربوية مباشرة، فإن موقف القرآن من الحس والعقل يفرض على التربية الإسلامية أن تجعل منهما معاً مصادر وطرائق رئيسة، وبذلك تتسم بالتكاملية في نظرتها الفلسفية.
كما أن الأطراف المعنية بالعملية التربوية مطالبة بالتزام هذه الفلسفة سواء في ذلك واضعو المناهج أم المعلمون والمتعلمون، أم الباحثون في مجال مناهج البحث العلمي. وبذلك تشق فلسفة التربية الإسلامية طريقها بين النظريات والأفكار التربوية المختلفة، بعيداً عن الثنائيات التي وقعت فيها معظم تلك النظريات والأفكار.
It affirms from another angle the connection of the intellect with the heart, and the connection of both together with the senses, for they are either all closed, or all open to truth, light, and guidance.
Finally, the disbelievers' disabling of their sensory and intellectual tools in this world necessitates that these tools receive a portion of punishment in the Hereafter:
'And We turn their hearts and their eyes away, as they did not believe in it the first time, and We leave them in their transgression wandering blindly' (Al-An'am 6:110).
'And they say, "If we had listened or used our intellect, we would not be among the companions of the Blaze."' (Al-Mulk 67:10). 'And We shall gather them on the Day of Resurrection on their faces, blind, dumb, and deaf' (Al-Isra 17:97).
'It is the fire of Allah, kindled, which ascends to the hearts' (Al-Humazah 104:6-7). And concerning the center of thought in the human brain, which is the forelock, He said: 'No! If he does not desist, We will surely seize him by the forelock, a lying, sinful forelock' (Al-Alaq 96:15-16).
The gist of the statement: The Quran does not see a clear contradiction between the functions of the senses and those of the intellect; rather, on the contrary, it sees positive interaction between them, complementarity and mutual support. As for claiming otherwise, it is a false illusion upon investigation. This is because certain intellectual knowledge is not fully realized, nor does it deserve this description, except when the intellect builds its judgments based on one of three things: either relying on one of the primary, innate, and definitive laws of the intellect, or relying on what provides certainty from sense testimonies, or relying on definitive transmitted testimonies. This third basis we will address in detail in what follows of this study - by Allah's permission.
Here it is necessary to consider the need to create a process of balance between perceptions. For innate balances - not acquired ones - are further from error than sense testimonies and the testimonies of others other than the infallible.
The intellect in this case becomes the touchstone for examining the perceptions of the senses and scrutinizing the testimonies of others and their reports about knowledge.
In light of that, the intellect classifies the knowledge coming to it from the two previous paths, placing each in its appropriate rank: certainty, preponderance, or what is below preponderance. Then rejection when it judges the impossibility or absurdity of what falls within the scope of its self-evident laws. Accordingly, not everything that reaches the intellectual apparatus is of one degree; rather, its highest is truth with certainty and its lowest is falsehood with certainty, and between them are preponderant conjecture, doubt, and weak conjecture - as was previously stated.
Regarding the direct relationship of that to the educational process, the Quran's stance on sense and intellect requires Islamic education to make both of them together main sources and methods, and thereby it is characterized by integration in its philosophical view.
Also, the parties involved in the educational process are required to adhere to this philosophy, whether curriculum developers, teachers and learners, or researchers in the field of scientific research methods. Thus, the philosophy of Islamic education carves its path among the various educational theories and ideas, far from the dualities in which most of those theories and ideas fell.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological exposition from a contemporary Islamic work arguing that the Quran presents an integrated view of intellect and senses, and then outlines the three bases of certain knowledge. The author first affirms the connection between intellect ('aql), heart (qalb), and senses (hawass), suggesting they operate together in receiving truth. He then cites Quranic verses showing that disbelievers will be punished in the afterlife for disabling their sensory and intellectual faculties in this world, implying these faculties are responsible for seeking truth. The core argument is that the Quran does not pit sense against reason but sees them as complementary. The author asserts that certain intellectual knowledge must be built on one of three foundations: (1) primary, innate, definitive laws of the intellect (e.g., the law of non-contradiction), (2) sense testimony that yields certainty, or (3) definitive transmitted testimony (mutawatir reports). This third basis is deferred to later. The author then discusses the need for balance, noting that innate balances are more reliable than sense testimony and reports from non-infallible sources. The intellect acts as a touchstone to examine sense perceptions and others' testimonies. It classifies incoming knowledge by degree—certainty, preponderant conjecture, doubt, weak conjecture—and rejects what contradicts self-evident laws. Finally, the author draws educational implications: Islamic education must treat both sense and intellect as sources and methods, avoiding the dualisms found in other educational philosophies. All examples are Quranic verses, fitting the Islamic intellectual tradition.

Key Terms
العقل
القلب
الحواس

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between the intellect, the heart, and the senses?
A) They are separate and often conflicting faculties.
B) They are connected and either all open or all closed to truth.
C) The intellect is superior and must suppress the senses.
D) The heart and senses are irrelevant to knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The Quran treats intellect, heart, and senses as an integrated system for acquiring truth, not as competing faculties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Read Quran 67:10 ('If we had listened or used our intellect…') and explain how this verse supports the author's claim that senses and intellect are complementary.
?
Identify a contemporary educational theory that separates reason from experience, and critique it using the Quranic integration principle from this passage.

The Three Foundations of Certain Knowledge in the Quranic Epistemology
Author outlines three bases for certain knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three bases of certain knowledge as described in the passage and explain why each provides certainty.

Core Idea
Certain knowledge rests on three pillars: innate reason, reliable senses, and definitive reports.

The passage declares that certain intellectual knowledge is only achieved when the intellect builds its judgments on one of three foundations. This lesson unpacks each foundation: primary innate laws of the intellect, sense testimonies yielding certainty, and definitive transmitted testimonies. Understanding these foundations is crucial for distinguishing certain from probable knowledge in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three bases in the Arabic
Search for the three phrases: 'القوانين العقلية الأولية الفطرية القطعية', 'ما يفيد اليقين من شهادات الحس', and 'الشهادات الخبرية القطعية'. These are the three bases.
The third is deferred to later in the study.
2
Define each basis concisely
The first refers to self-evident a priori principles like the law of non-contradiction. The second refers to sensory evidence that is unmistakable (e.g., seeing fire). The third refers to reports that are mutawatir, i.e., transmitted by so many that certainty is attained.
Classical Islamic epistemology often uses 'badahiyyat' for the first, 'mushahadat' for the second, and 'mutawatirat' for the third.
3
Explain why each yields certainty
Primary intellectual laws are necessarily true by their nature; sense testimonies can yield certainty when conditions like proper functioning and absence of illusion are met; transmitted testimonies yield certainty when they reach the level of tawatur (unbroken chains of reliable narrators).
The author emphasizes that this is the only way intellectual certainty is realized.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
In Usul al-Fiqh, a ruling may be known with certainty through a mutawatir hadith (third basis), or through qiyas on a clear 'illah derived from primary principles (first basis), or through direct observation of a legal cause (second basis).
For instance, the prohibition of riba is known via mutawatir texts.

وأخيراً فإن تعطيل الكفار لأدواتهم الحسية والعقلية في الدنيا يستوجب أن تنال هذه الأدوات قسطاً من العقاب في الآخرة:

وهنا يراعى ضرورة إحداث عملية موازنة بين المدركات. فالموازين الفطرية - وليس المكتسبة - أبعد الخطأ من شهادات الحس وشهادات الآخرين غير عن المعصومين.

The Intellect as Touchstone: Classifying Knowledge and Avoiding Error
Author describes intellect's classificatory and critical function.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the role of the intellect as a touchstone for classifying knowledge into degrees of certainty and rejecting falsehood.

Core Idea
The intellect judges and ranks knowledge by certainty, rejecting what contradicts self-evident laws.

After presenting the three foundations, the passage explains how the intellect processes the knowledge derived from sense and testimony. It acts as a touchstone (al-muḥakk) that examines and classifies perceptions, assigns each its proper rank (certainty, preponderant conjecture, doubt, weak conjecture), and rejects what is impossible or absurd according to its self-evident laws. This lesson explores this critical function of the intellect in Quranic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Find the touchstone metaphor in Arabic
Locate the phrase 'العقل في هذه الحال المحك لفحص مدركات الحس'. This directly compares the intellect to a touchstone used to test gold.
The term 'muḥakk' is a tool for distinguishing genuine from false; here the intellect distinguishes true knowledge from false.
2
Identify the classification of knowledge degrees
The passage lists: certainty (yaqīn), preponderant conjecture (ẓann rājiḥ), doubt (shakk), and weak conjecture (ẓann marjūḥ). Also mentioned are truth with certainty and falsehood with certainty.
These degrees are common in Islamic epistemology, especially in uṣūl al-fiqh.
3
Understand the rejection criterion
The intellect rejects any perception or report that contradicts its self-evident laws (qawānīn dhātiyya). For example, a report claiming that something both exists and does not exist simultaneously would be rejected as absurd.
This corresponds to the law of non-contradiction, one of the primary intellectual laws.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
In ʿilm al-kalām, a mutakallim uses the intellect to reject certain theological claims. For instance, if someone claims that Allah is corporeal, the intellect judges this as impossible because it contradicts self-evident truths about the divine (e.g., tanzih). The intellect classifies such a claim as false with certainty.
This shows the intellect's role in demarcating true belief from heresy.

وهنا يراعى ضرورة إحداث عملية موازنة بين المدركات. فالموازين الفطرية - وليس المكتسبة - أبعد الخطأ من شهادات الحس وشهادات الآخرين غير عن المعصومين.
العقل في هذه الحال المحك لفحص مدركات الحس ويصبح وتمحيص شهادات الآخرين، وأخبارهم عن المعارف.
وفي ضوء ذلك يقوم العقل بتصنيف المعارف القادمة إليه من الطريقين السابقين لوضع كل منهما في رتبته المناسبة يقيناً أو ترجيحاً، أو ما هو دون الرجحان. ثم الرفض حال حكمه باستحالة أو امتناع ما يدخل في نطاق قوانينه الذاتية. وعليه فليس كل ما ورد إلى الجهاز الفكري المدرك في درجة واحدة، بل أعلاها الحق بيقين وأدناها الباطل بيقين وبينهما الظن الراجح، والشك والظن المرجوح - كما سبق القول فيما مضى.
وبالنسبة إلى علاقة ذلك بالعملية التربوية مباشرة، فإن موقف القرآن من الحس والعقل يفرض على التربية الإسلامية أن تجعل منهما معاً مصادر وطرائق رئيسة، وبذلك تتسم بالتكاملية في نظرتها الفلسفية.
كما أن الأطراف المعنية بالعملية التربوية مطالبة بالتزام هذه الفلسفة سواء في ذلك واضعو المناهج أم المعلمون والمتعلمون، أم الباحثون في مجال مناهج البحث العلمي. وبذلك تشق فلسفة التربية الإسلامية طريقها بين النظريات والأفكار التربوية المختلفة، بعيداً عن الثنائيات التي وقعت فيها معظم تلك النظريات والأفكار.
It affirms from another angle the connection of the intellect with the heart, and the connection of both together with the senses, for they are either all closed, or all open to truth, light, and guidance.
Finally, the disbelievers' disabling of their sensory and intellectual tools in this world necessitates that these tools receive a portion of punishment in the Hereafter:
'And We turn their hearts and their eyes away, as they did not believe in it the first time, and We leave them in their transgression wandering blindly' (Al-An'am 6:110).
'And they say, "If we had listened or used our intellect, we would not be among the companions of the Blaze."' (Al-Mulk 67:10). 'And We shall gather them on the Day of Resurrection on their faces, blind, dumb, and deaf' (Al-Isra 17:97).
'It is the fire of Allah, kindled, which ascends to the hearts' (Al-Humazah 104:6-7). And concerning the center of thought in the human brain, which is the forelock, He said: 'No! If he does not desist, We will surely seize him by the forelock, a lying, sinful forelock' (Al-Alaq 96:15-16).
The gist of the statement: The Quran does not see a clear contradiction between the functions of the senses and those of the intellect; rather, on the contrary, it sees positive interaction between them, complementarity and mutual support. As for claiming otherwise, it is a false illusion upon investigation. This is because certain intellectual knowledge is not fully realized, nor does it deserve this description, except when the intellect builds its judgments based on one of three things: either relying on one of the primary, innate, and definitive laws of the intellect, or relying on what provides certainty from sense testimonies, or relying on definitive transmitted testimonies. This third basis we will address in detail in what follows of this study - by Allah's permission.
Here it is necessary to consider the need to create a process of balance between perceptions. For innate balances - not acquired ones - are further from error than sense testimonies and the testimonies of others other than the infallible.
The intellect in this case becomes the touchstone for examining the perceptions of the senses and scrutinizing the testimonies of others and their reports about knowledge.
In light of that, the intellect classifies the knowledge coming to it from the two previous paths, placing each in its appropriate rank: certainty, preponderance, or what is below preponderance. Then rejection when it judges the impossibility or absurdity of what falls within the scope of its self-evident laws. Accordingly, not everything that reaches the intellectual apparatus is of one degree; rather, its highest is truth with certainty and its lowest is falsehood with certainty, and between them are preponderant conjecture, doubt, and weak conjecture - as was previously stated.
Regarding the direct relationship of that to the educational process, the Quran's stance on sense and intellect requires Islamic education to make both of them together main sources and methods, and thereby it is characterized by integration in its philosophical view.
Also, the parties involved in the educational process are required to adhere to this philosophy, whether curriculum developers, teachers and learners, or researchers in the field of scientific research methods. Thus, the philosophy of Islamic education carves its path among the various educational theories and ideas, far from the dualities in which most of those theories and ideas fell.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological exposition from a contemporary Islamic work arguing that the Quran presents an integrated view of intellect and senses, and then outlines the three bases of certain knowledge. The author first affirms the connection between intellect ('aql), heart (qalb), and senses (hawass), suggesting they operate together in receiving truth. He then cites Quranic verses showing that disbelievers will be punished in the afterlife for disabling their sensory and intellectual faculties in this world, implying these faculties are responsible for seeking truth. The core argument is that the Quran does not pit sense against reason but sees them as complementary. The author asserts that certain intellectual knowledge must be built on one of three foundations: (1) primary, innate, definitive laws of the intellect (e.g., the law of non-contradiction), (2) sense testimony that yields certainty, or (3) definitive transmitted testimony (mutawatir reports). This third basis is deferred to later. The author then discusses the need for balance, noting that innate balances are more reliable than sense testimony and reports from non-infallible sources. The intellect acts as a touchstone to examine sense perceptions and others' testimonies. It classifies incoming knowledge by degree—certainty, preponderant conjecture, doubt, weak conjecture—and rejects what contradicts self-evident laws. Finally, the author draws educational implications: Islamic education must treat both sense and intellect as sources and methods, avoiding the dualisms found in other educational philosophies. All examples are Quranic verses, fitting the Islamic intellectual tradition.

Key Terms
المحك
التصنيف
اليقين
الظن الراجح
الشك
الظن المرجوح

Key Terms from Source Passage

The Role of Intellect in Classifying Knowledge
Author explains how intellect balances sensory and reported knowledge and classifies them by certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five epistemic ranks (yaqin, zann rajih, shakk, zann marjuh, and batil) and classify a given piece of knowledge according to the intellect's judgment as described in the passage.

Core Idea
The intellect classifies all knowledge into a hierarchy of certainty using innate scales.

In this passage, the author explains how the intellect ('aql) does not passively receive knowledge but actively balances and classifies it. Using innate scales (mawazin fitriyya), the intellect examines sensory data and reports from others, then assigns each piece of knowledge to an appropriate rank: certainty, preponderant conjecture, doubt, weak conjecture, or falsehood. This classification is essential for understanding how Islamic epistemology distinguishes between reliable and unreliable knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sources of knowledge
Find the two sources mentioned: sensory percepts (madrakat al-hiss) and testimonies of others (shahadat al-akharin). The intellect processes both.
Sensory knowledge includes direct observation; reported knowledge comes from other people, including non-infallibles.
2
Distinguish innate from acquired scales
The author emphasizes that innate scales (al-mawazin al-fitriyya), not acquired ones, are furthest from error. Understand why innate criteria are foundational for judgment.
Innate scales are built into human nature; acquired scales may be corrupted by custom or bias.
3
List the five epistemic ranks
From highest to lowest: truth with certainty (haqq bi-yaqin), preponderant conjecture (zann rajih), doubt (shakk), weak conjecture (zann marjuh), and falsehood with certainty (batil bi-yaqin).
The passage also mentions rejection when something is impossible or inadmissible.
4
Classify a sample knowledge item
Take a sensory observation (e.g., seeing the crescent moon for Ramadan) and a report from a single narrator (khabar wahid). Determine which rank each falls into using the passage's criteria.
Sensory observation can yield certainty if clear and consistent; a solitary report typically gives zann rajih or weaker.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهنا يراعى ضرورة إحداث عملية موازنة بين المدركات. فالموازين الفطرية وليس المكتسبة - أبعد الخطأ من شهادات الحس وشهادات الآخرين غير عن المعصومين. العقل في هذه الحال المحك لفحص مدركات الجس ويصبح وتمحيص شهادات الآخرين، وأخبارهم عن المعارف. وفي ضوء ذلك يقوم العقل بتصنيف المعارف القادمة إليه من الطريقين السابقين لوضع كل منهما في رتبته المناسبة يقيناً أو ترجيحاً، أو ما هو دون الرجحان. ثم الرفض حال حكمه باستحالة أو امتناع ما يدخل في نطاق قوانينه الذاتية. وعليه فليس كل ما ورد إلى الجهاز الفكري المدرك في درجة واحدة، بل أعلاها الحق بيقين وأدناها الباطل بيقين وبينهما الظن الراجح، والشك والظن المرجوح (١) - كما سبق القول فيما مضى.
And here it is necessary to create a process of balancing between percepts. So the innate scales, not acquired, are furthest from error regarding the testimonies of sense and the testimonies of others other than the infallibles. In this case, the intellect is the criterion to examine the percepts of the senses and becomes a means of scrutinizing the testimonies of others and their reports about knowledge. In light of that, the intellect classifies the knowledge arriving to it from the two previous paths, placing each in its appropriate rank, whether certainty or preponderance, or what is below preponderance. Then rejection when it judges the impossibility or inadmissibility of what enters within the scope of its intrinsic laws. Accordingly, not everything that comes to the perceiving intellectual apparatus is of one degree, but the highest is truth with certainty and the lowest is falsehood with certainty, and between them are preponderant conjecture, doubt, and weak conjecture (1) – as previously stated.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion on the role of intellect ('aql) in classifying knowledge. The author begins by stating the need to balance perceptions. He distinguishes between innate (fitri) and acquired scales, asserting that innate scales are more accurate for sensory testimonies and reports from non-infallible sources. Here, 'infallibles' likely refers to prophets and imams. The intellect becomes the criterion (mahakk) to examine sensory percepts and to scrutinize others' testimonies and reports. Then the intellect classifies knowledge from these two sources (sense and report) into ranks: certainty (yaqin), preponderance (tarjih), below preponderance, and also rejects what is impossible or inadmissible according to its inherent laws. The passage concludes that knowledge is not of one degree: the highest is truth with certainty, the lowest is falsehood with certainty, and in between are preponderant conjecture (zann rajih), doubt (shakk), and weak conjecture (zann marjuh). This classification is foundational for Islamic epistemology—for example, certainty corresponds to knowledge from mutawatir reports or clear proofs, while conjecture is like a weak hadith or ambiguous evidence.

Key Terms
المدركات (al-mudrakat): Percepts or things perceived, including sensory and intellectual data. [Note: No significant shift; stable term in epistemology.]
الموازين الفطرية (al-mawazin al-fitriyya): Innate scales or criteria inherent in human nature for judging truth. [Note: Often contrasted with acquired scales; used similarly in Islamic epistemology.]
العقل (al-'aql): The intellect as the criterion for examining sensory percepts and classifying knowledge. [Note: In this text, 'aql is the active faculty of judgment; in logic texts (mantiq), 'aql is sometimes more narrowly rational faculty.]
اليقين (al-yaqin): Certainty, the highest degree of knowledge that is true without doubt. [Note: Stable term in Islamic epistemology.]
الظن الراجح (al-zann al-rajih): Preponderant conjecture, strong probability outweighing doubt. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, zann rajih is the basis for ijtihad; here it's an epistemic category.]
الشك (al-shakk): Doubt, balanced between two possibilities without preponderance. [Note: Standard term.]
الظن المرجوح (al-zann al-marjuh): Weak conjecture, probability outweighed by doubt. [Note: Less common; typically zann is divided into rajih and marjuh in some epistemologies.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the intellect do with knowledge coming from sense and reports?
A) Accepts all equally
B) Rejects all
C) Classifies it into ranks of certainty, conjecture, etc.
D) Only deals with sensory data

Key Takeaway
The intellect, using innate scales, classifies all knowledge into a hierarchy from certainty to falsehood, with intermediate ranks of conjecture and doubt.

Definition of Revelation (Wahy) – Linguistic and Terminological
Author defines wahy linguistically and terminologically, citing classical lexicons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the linguistic and terminological definitions of wahy and explain the contrast between ishara and 'ibara.

Core Idea
Wahy is divine swift indication that directly embodies the thing indicated, not ordinary expression.

Revelation (wahy) is the foundation of Islamic knowledge. Yet what exactly does the term mean? In this passage, the author unpacks the word's linguistic roots and technical usage in the Islamic sciences, showing how it differs from ordinary expression. Understanding this definition is crucial for any discourse on prophethood and the divine origin of the Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two dimensions of the definition
The passage first gives the linguistic (lugha) definition, then the terminological (istilah) definition. Mark where each begins.
The linguistic definition focuses on the root meaning of swift indication; the terminological specifies the divine message.
2
Contrast ishara and 'ibara
The text distinguishes ishara (indication) from 'ibara (expression). Ishara is the thing indicated itself; 'ibara allows transition from word to meaning. Understand why revelation is ishara not 'ibara.
This contrast is key to why wahy is not ordinary speech.
3
Trace the origin of wahy
The author states the origin is 'swift indication'. Consider how this connects to the various modes of revelation (e.g., angelic, direct) mentioned in the Qur'an.
Swiftness implies immediacy and hiddenness from others.
4
Apply the definition to the Qur'an
The Qur'an is the prime example of wahy. Check whether it satisfies both the linguistic and terminological definitions.
The Qur'an is divine speech sent to a prophet, and it is direct indication of meaning without human mediation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف الوحي. الوحي لغة: وحي إليه وله كلمه بكلام يخفى على غيره، وأوحى إليه وله أشار وأوما). والوحي: ما يقع به الإشارة القائمة مقام العبارة من غير عبارة، فإن العبارة يجوز منها إلى المعنى المقصود بها، ولذا سميت عبارة، بخلاف الإشارة التي هي الوحي فإنها ذات المشار إليه))(٢). وأصل الوحي: الإشارة السريعة، ويكون بطرق مختلفة(۳). كما سيردنا من خلال النصوص القرآنية. وفي الاصطلاح الوحي: الكلمة الإلهية التي تلقى إلى أنبيائه (٤). وبتعبير أشمل (فالوحي كلام الله تعالى المنزل على نبي من أنبيائه، وهو تعريف له بمعنى اسم المفعول أي الموحى) .
Definition of revelation. Revelation linguistically: wahi ilayhi wa lahu kalimahu bi kalam yakhfa 'ala ghayrihi, and awha ilayhi wa lahu ashara wa auma). And revelation: that by which indication that stands in the place of expression without expression occurs, for expression allows transition to the intended meaning, therefore it is called expression, unlike indication which is revelation, for it has the very thing indicated) (2). The origin of revelation is swift indication, and it occurs in various ways (3). As will come to us through Qur'anic texts. And terminologically, revelation: the divine word that is communicated to His prophets (4). And in a more comprehensive expression: (revelation is the speech of Allah Almighty sent down to a prophet from among His prophets, and it is a definition in the sense of the passive participle, i.e., that which is revealed).
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a definition of revelation (al-wahy). The author begins with the linguistic definition: the verb 'yuhi' means to speak to someone with speech hidden from others, or to signal. He cites a lexicographical source that distinguishes revelation as indication (ishara) that takes the place of expression but is not expression itself, because expression allows passing to the intended meaning, whereas revelation (indication) directly embodies the indicated. The origin of revelation is swift indication. This is followed by the terminological definition: revelation is the divine word communicated to prophets. A more comprehensive definition adds that revelation is Allah's speech sent down to a prophet, and it is a definition in the sense of the passive participle (ism maf'ul), i.e., 'that which is revealed'. Throughout, the author relies on classical sources such as al-Firuzabadi and al-Raghib al-Isfahani. The Islamic example: the revelation of the Qur'an to Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) is the prime example of wahy.

Key Terms
الوحي
الإشارة
العبارة

Wahy is divine swift indication that communicates Allah's speech to prophets, distinct from ordinary expression and defined both linguistically and terminologically in the Islamic tradition.

Refuting the Flawed Sensory Qiyas
Refutation of a sensory qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and critique the invalid syllogism that claims sensory dormancy implies lack of perception.

Core Idea
Not every qiyas is valid – empirical reality refutes it.

The author presents a deductive argument that attempts to prove that if the senses do not perceive something when active, they certainly will not perceive it when dormant. He then exposes this argument as invalid because it is contradicted by actual experience and observation. This lesson trains you to detect such flawed arguments in theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
Locate the statement that the sensory faculties are causes of perception, and the conclusion that someone who does not perceive with active senses is even less likely to perceive when senses are dormant.
The claim is presented as a 'qiyas' but the author will refute it.
2
Extract the qiyas structure
Formalize the argument: Premise 1: Sensory faculties are causes of perception. Premise 2: If a cause is present and perception does not occur, then perception is even more unlikely when the cause is absent/dormant. Conclusion: Therefore, anyone who does not perceive with senses present will not perceive when senses are dormant.
This is a form of a fortiori argument (qiyas awla).
3
Show why it is invalid
The author says 'existence and observation belie' this argument. This means there are real examples where perception happens when senses are dormant (e.g., dreams, intuition). Thus the premise is empirically false.
In Islamic epistemology, dreams are considered a form of perception beyond the senses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استحالته، وقال: القوى الحاسة أسباب الإدراك، فمن لم يدرك الشيء مع وجودها وحضورها، فبأن لا يدرك مع ركودها أولى وأحق !! وهذا نوع قياس يكذبه الوجود والمشاهدة، فكما أن العقل طور من أطوار الآدمي، يحصل فيه عين يبصر بها أنواعاً من المعقولات الحواس معزولة عنها، فالنبوة أيضاً عبارة عن طور يحصل فيه عين لها نور يظهر في نورها الغيب وأمور لا يدركها العقل. وإذا أيقنا بضعف الحواس والعقل عن إدراك حقيقة النبوة، فما عاد هناك سوى الخبر اليقيني ممثلاً في الوحي.
Its impossibility: He said: The sensory faculties are causes of perception. So whoever does not perceive a thing while they exist and are present, then that he does not perceive when they are dormant is more fitting and more worthy!! And this is a type of syllogism that existence and observation belie. For just as the intellect is a stage of the human stages, in which an eye occurs that sees by it types of intelligibles, the senses being isolated from it, so prophetship is also an expression of a stage in which an eye occurs that has light; in its light appear the unseen and things that the intellect does not perceive. And when we are certain of the weakness of the senses and the intellect to perceive the reality of prophetship, there remains nothing except certain report represented by revelation.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against those who claim that if the senses do not perceive something when they are active, they are even less likely to perceive it when they are dormant. The author refutes this by calling it a flawed syllogism (qiyas) that is contradicted by actual existence and observation. He then provides an analogy: just as the intellect is a separate stage (tawr) in the human being with its own inner eye that perceives rational concepts independent of the senses, similarly prophethood (nubuwwa) is a separate stage with an inner eye that has light, by which the unseen (ghayb) and matters beyond the intellect are perceived. The conclusion is that once we realize the weakness of senses and intellect regarding the reality of prophethood, only certain news (khabar yaqini) remains, which is the revelation (al-wahi). Note that the author uses the term 'qiyas' here to refer to a deductive argument that he considers invalid. The analogy with the intellect is used to justify the possibility of prophethood as a distinct cognitive faculty. This passage is foundational for establishing revelation as the only certain source of knowledge about prophethood.

Key Terms
الطور
العين
الخبر اليقيني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author use to refute the flawed sensory qiyas?
A) Logical deduction
B) Empirical observation and existence
C) Consensus of scholars
D) Revelation from the Quran

Key Takeaway
A flawed qiyas can be refuted by showing that its premise contradicts observable reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Analogy for Prophecy as a Distinct Faculty
Analogy for prophecy as a separate faculty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the analogical argument that establishes prophecy as a separate cognitive stage beyond sense and intellect.

Core Idea
Prophecy is to the unseen what intellect is to the intelligible.

Having refuted the flawed sensory argument, the author now provides a positive analogy. He compares the relationship between intellect and intelligibles to the relationship between prophethood and the unseen. Just as the intellect is a developmental stage with an inner eye that perceives abstract concepts independently of the senses, prophethood is a stage with an inner eye that perceives the unseen. This lesson teaches you to analyze analogical arguments in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogate (the known)
The author uses the intellect ('aql) as the known term. The intellect is a stage (tawr) of human development that has an 'inner eye' (al-'ayn) that perceives intelligibles (al-ma'qulat) independently of the senses.
This is a common theme in Islamic philosophy: the intellect abstracts universals from particulars.
2
Identify the analogue (the unknown)
The unknown is prophethood (nubuwwa). The author claims it is also a stage that has an inner eye with a special light that perceives the unseen (ghayb) and matters beyond the intellect.
The light (nur) is a metaphor for prophetic inspiration.
3
Determine the ground of analogy
The ground is that both are stages of human existence that possess a distinct faculty of perception (the 'inner eye') that operates on a different domain: the intellect on intelligibles, prophethood on the unseen.
The analogy relies on the similarity of structure: each stage has its own 'ayn.
4
Evaluate the strength of the analogy
The analogy is plausible if one accepts that the intellect exists and functions as described. The author assumes the reader acknowledges the intellect's independence from senses. The strength lies in the parallel: both are faculties that access domains inaccessible to lower stages.
This is not a strict logical proof but an argument for the possibility of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استحالته، وقال: القوى الحاسة أسباب الإدراك، فمن لم يدرك الشيء مع وجودها وحضورها، فبأن لا يدرك مع ركودها أولى وأحق !! وهذا نوع قياس يكذبه الوجود والمشاهدة، فكما أن العقل طور من أطوار الآدمي، يحصل فيه عين يبصر بها أنواعاً من المعقولات الحواس معزولة عنها، فالنبوة أيضاً عبارة عن طور يحصل فيه عين لها نور يظهر في نورها الغيب وأمور لا يدركها العقل. وإذا أيقنا بضعف الحواس والعقل عن إدراك حقيقة النبوة، فما عاد هناك سوى الخبر اليقيني ممثلاً في الوحي.
Its impossibility: He said: The sensory faculties are causes of perception. So whoever does not perceive a thing while they exist and are present, then that he does not perceive when they are dormant is more fitting and more worthy!! And this is a type of syllogism that existence and observation belie. For just as the intellect is a stage of the human stages, in which an eye occurs that sees by it types of intelligibles, the senses being isolated from it, so prophetship is also an expression of a stage in which an eye occurs that has light; in its light appear the unseen and things that the intellect does not perceive. And when we are certain of the weakness of the senses and the intellect to perceive the reality of prophetship, there remains nothing except certain report represented by revelation.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against those who claim that if the senses do not perceive something when they are active, they are even less likely to perceive it when they are dormant. The author refutes this by calling it a flawed syllogism (qiyas) that is contradicted by actual existence and observation. He then provides an analogy: just as the intellect is a separate stage (tawr) in the human being with its own inner eye that perceives rational concepts independent of the senses, similarly prophethood (nubuwwa) is a separate stage with an inner eye that has light, by which the unseen (ghayb) and matters beyond the intellect are perceived. The conclusion is that once we realize the weakness of senses and intellect regarding the reality of prophethood, only certain news (khabar yaqini) remains, which is the revelation (al-wahi). Note that the author uses the term 'qiyas' here to refer to a deductive argument that he considers invalid. The analogy with the intellect is used to justify the possibility of prophethood as a distinct cognitive faculty. This passage is foundational for establishing revelation as the only certain source of knowledge about prophethood.

العين
الخبر اليقيني

Key Takeaway
Analogy is used to establish the possibility of a higher faculty of perception, making room for prophetic knowledge beyond reason.

?
Evaluate the author's analogy: what are its limitations? Could someone reject the existence of the intellect's inner eye?

The Certainty and Supremacy of Revelation
Author cites Quranic verses to affirm certainty and supremacy of revelation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the certainty of revelation (qat'iyya al-wurud) is established from the Quran itself and why revelation takes precedence over other sources of knowledge.

Core Idea
Revelation is certain because it comes from the All-Knowing God; all other knowledge is relative.

This lesson examines the Quranic basis for the certainty of revelation. The author argues that revelation itself declares its own certainty, and God's omniscience guarantees its truth. Understanding this hierarchy is crucial for distinguishing between true knowledge and superstitious claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the first sentence and the conclusion. The author asserts that revelation (al-wahi) is a text that states its own certainty and categorical occurrence (qat'iyya al-wurudiyya), and that it dominates sense, intellect, and all other sources.
The claim is dual: certainty and supremacy.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
Look at the verses cited: Al-Baqarah 2:23 and Yunus 10:94 challenge doubters; Al-Baqarah 2:176 identifies God as the source; Al-Baqarah 2:7, Al-Imran 3:5, Al-An'am 6:59, Saba' 34:3 affirm God's comprehensive knowledge. These verses collectively establish the revelation's truth and the source's omniscience.
The verses serve both as proof and as examples of the revelation's own testimony.
3
Understand the epistemological hierarchy
From God's omniscience, the author deduces that all human knowledge is relative and only a small part of God's knowledge (Al-Baqarah 2:255, Al-Isra' 17:85, Yusuf 12:76). This places revelation at the top, since it comes directly from God.
Human knowledge is limited; revelation provides certain knowledge beyond human reach.
4
Apply the conclusion to reject false sources
The author concludes that the path of prophethood alone is the legitimate source of certain knowledge, protecting against superstitions like sorcery, astrology, etc. This 'scientific thinking' is based on revelation.
The term 'scientific thinking' here means methodical inquiry rooted in revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الوحي نص على يقينية معرفته وقطعيتها الورودية في مثل قول الله تعالى: ﴿وَإِن كُنتُمْ فِي رَيْبٍ مِمَّا نَزَّلْنَا عَلَى عَبْدِنَا فَأْتُوا بِسُورَةٍ مِنْ مِثْلِهِ وَادْعُوا شُهَدَاءَكُمْ مِنْ دُونِ اللّهِ إِن كُنتُمْ صادِقِينَ ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٣/٢ ]. فإن كُنتَ فِي شَكٍّ مِمَّا أَنْزَلْنَا إِلَيْكَ فَاسْأَلِ الَّذِينَ يَقْرَؤُونَ الْكِتَابَ مِنْ قَبْلِكَ لَقَدْ جَاءَكَ الْحَقُّ مِنْ رَبِّكَ فَلا تَكُونَنَّ مِنَ الْمُمْتَرِينَ﴾ [يونس: ٩٤/١٠]. ولا غرو في هذا فإن مصدر الوحي هو الله - تعالى-: ﴿ذَلِكَ بِأَنَّ اللهَ نَزَّلَ الْكِتابَ بِالْحَقِّ ﴾ [البقرة: ١٧٦/٢]. وإذا كان الله - تعالى - مصدر الوحي (القرآن) فإنه – سبحانه – مصدر المعرفة بالمعنى العام المطلق كذلك: أَوَلا يَعْلَمُونَ أَنَّ اللَّهَ يَعْلَمُ ما يُسِرُّونَ وَمَا يُعْلِنُونَ ﴾ [البقرة: ٧/٢]. ويعلم حتى الجزئيات وجزيئاتها: وإنَّ اللَّهَ لا يَخْفَى عَلَيْهِ شَيْءٌ فِي الْأَرْضِ وَلَا فِي السَّماءِ﴾ [آل عمران: ٥/٣] وَيَعْلَمُ مَا فِي الْبَرِّ وَالْبَحْرِ وَمَا تَسْقُطُ مِنْ وَرَقَةٍ إِلَّا يَعْلَمُها وَلَا حَبَّةٍ فِي ظُلُماتِ الْأَرْضِ وَلَا رَطْبٍ وَلا يَابِسٍ إِلا في كتاب مبين﴾ [الأنعام: ٥٩/٦]. عالِم الْغَيْبِ لا يَعْزُبُ عَنْهُ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ فِي السَّمَاواتِ وَلَا فِي الْأَرْضِ وَلَا أَصْغَرُ مِنْ ذَلِكَ وَلَا أَكْبَرُ إِلَّا فِي كِتَابٍ مُبِين﴾ [سبأ: ٣/٣٤]. وكل ما عندنا من معرفة فهي نسبية لا تمثل سوى جزء لا يتجزأ من علم الله الواسع المحيط، ولا ننالها إلا بمشيئته سبحانه ... وَلَا يُحِيطُونَ بِشَيْءٍ مِنْ عِلْمِهِ إلا بما شاء... [البقرة: ٢٥٥/٢]. ... وَما أُوتِيتُمْ مِنَ الْعِلْمِ إِلا قَلِيلاً ... [الإسراء: ٨٥/١٧]. ﴿وَفَوْقَ كُلِّ ذِي عِلْمٍ عَلِيمٍ﴾ [يوسف: ٧٦/١٢]. وإذا تقرر ذلك فإن هيمنة الوحي على كل من الحس والعقل - فضلاً عن المصادر التبعية الأخرى - تجعل من طريق النبوة وحدها وحياً يمتلك تلك القوة اليقينية، سواء في وروده أم في بعض دلالته وبذلك تتحصن عقول الناشئة ومسالكهم من أي ادعاءات تزعم أن لها صلة بالعالم العلوي عن طريق آخر كالشعوذة أو التنجيم والكهانة والعرافة.. إلخ ضروب التفكير الخرافي. ويحل محلها التفكير العلمي المعتمد على مصدر النبوة وطريقها وحدها منهجاً مشروعاً.
And this revelation is a text regarding the certainty of its knowledge and its categorical occurrence, such as in the saying of God Most High: 'And if you are in doubt about what We have sent down upon Our servant, then produce a surah of the likes thereof and call upon your witnesses besides God, if you are truthful.' [Al-Baqarah 2:23]. 'Then if you are in doubt about what We have sent down to you, ask those who read the Book before you. Surely the truth has come to you from your Lord, so do not be among the doubters.' [Yunus 10:94]. And there is no wonder in this, for the source of revelation is God Most High: 'That is because God has sent down the Book with truth.' [Al-Baqarah 2:176]. And if God Most High is the source of revelation (the Quran), then He, glorified, is the source of knowledge in the absolute general sense as well: 'Do they not know that God knows what they conceal and what they declare?' [Al-Baqarah 2:7]. And He knows even the particulars and their details: 'Indeed, nothing is hidden from God in the earth or in the heaven.' [Al-Imran 3:5]. 'And He knows what is in the land and sea; no leaf falls but He knows it, and no grain in the darkness of the earth, and no fresh or dry thing but is in a clear book.' [Al-An'am 6:59]. 'Knower of the unseen; not an atom's weight escapes Him in the heavens or the earth, nor anything smaller than that or larger, except it is in a clear book.' [Saba' 34:3]. And all knowledge we have is relative, representing only an inseparable part of God's vast encompassing knowledge, and we only attain it by His will... 'And they encompass not anything of His knowledge except what He wills...' [Al-Baqarah 2:255]. '... and you have not been given of knowledge except a little.' [Al-Isra' 17:85]. 'And above every possessor of knowledge is One All-Knowing.' [Yusuf 12:76]. And when that is established, then the dominance of revelation over both sense and intellect – not to mention other secondary sources – makes the path of prophethood alone a revelation possessing that certain power, whether in its occurrence or in some of its indication. And thereby the minds and paths of the youth are fortified from any claims that allege connection to the higher world through another way, like sorcery, astrology, soothsaying, divination, etc., forms of superstitious thinking. And it is replaced by scientific thinking based on the source of prophethood and its path alone as a legitimate methodology.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological argument for the certainty and supremacy of revelation. The author begins by asserting that revelation (al-wahi) itself is a text (nass) that declares its own certain knowledge and categorical occurrence (qat'iyya al-wurudiyya). He then supports this with several Quranic verses. The first two verses (Al-Baqarah 2:23 and Yunus 10:94) challenge anyone in doubt to produce something like the Quran or ask the People of the Book, thereby establishing the truth and certainty of the revelation. Next, the author states that it is no wonder because the source of revelation is God Himself, quoting Al-Baqarah 2:176. He then expands on God's omniscience, citing verses that affirm God knows all, even the smallest details (Al-Baqarah 2:7, Al-Imran 3:5, Al-An'am 6:59, Saba' 34:3). From this, the author draws a crucial epistemological principle: all human knowledge is relative and only a small part of God's knowledge; we only know what He wills (Al-Baqarah 2:255, Al-Isra' 17:85, Yusuf 12:76). This establishes a hierarchy: revelation (from God) is at the top, and everything else is derivative. The conclusion is that revelation dominates sense and intellect and all other sources. Therefore, the path of prophethood alone possesses certain power, both in its occurrence (wurud) and in its indication (dalala). This protects the youth from false claims like sorcery, astrology, etc., which are superstitious. In their place, the author advocates for 'scientific thinking' (al-tafkir al-'ilmi) based on the source of prophethood alone as a legitimate methodology. Note that the term 'scientific thinking' here is used in contrast to superstitious thinking, not in the modern secular sense. The author is arguing that true science is based on revelation.

Key Terms
الوحي
القطعية الورودية
الهيمنة

Key Takeaway
Revelation is the only source of certain knowledge because it originates from the All-Knowing God, and its certainty is affirmed by the Quran itself.

The Purpose of Revelation: Glad Tidings and Warning
Author states the main purpose of revelation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main purposes of revelation as derived from the Quranic texts and explain how they function as a definition of revelation's goal.

Core Idea
Revelation serves to bring hope and caution, and to transform lives from ignorance to guidance.

In this passage, the author concludes his discussion on revelation by clarifying its ultimate purpose. He draws directly from the Quran to show that revelation is not merely informative but transformative: it brings glad tidings and warning, and leads humanity from darkness to light. This lesson will help you grasp how the author defines the purpose of revelation using two key Quranic proof texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The author defines 'ghaya al-wahy' (purpose of revelation). Note that he does not give a single sentence definition but points to Quranic verses.
2
Extract the two purposes from the verses
From Surah Saba' (34:28) we get 'bishara wa indhar' (glad tidings and warning). From Surah Ibrahim (14:1) we get 'ikhraj min al-zulumat ila al-nur' (bringing from darkness to light).
3
Analyze the relationship between the two purposes
The first is about communication (good news and warning), the second about transformation (from ignorance to knowledge). Are they separate or complementary?
4
Assess how these purposes constitute a definition
A definition of revelation's purpose should cover both aspects. The author uses these two verses to encapsulate the core function.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غاية الوحي وبوسعنا إدراك غاية الوحي من جملة النصوص التالية: البشارة والإنذار كغاية رئيسة ﴿وَما أَرْسَلْنَاكَ إِلا كافةً لِلنَّاسِ بَشِيرًا وَنَذِيراً (سبا: ٢٨/٣٤]. وإخراج الناس من الظلمات إلى النور: الر كِتابٌ أَنْزَلْنَاهُ إِلَيْكَ لِتُخْرِجَ النَّاسَ مِنَ الظُّلُمَاتِ إِلَى النُّورِ بِإِذْنِ رَبِّهِمْ... [إبراهيم: ١/١٤].
The purpose of revelation: And we can perceive the purpose of revelation from the following texts: Glad tidings and warning as a main purpose. 'And We have not sent you except comprehensively to mankind as a bearer of good news and a warner.' [Saba' 34:28]. And bringing people out of darkness into light: 'Alif Lam Ra. A Book which We have sent down to you to bring people out of the darkness into the light by permission of their Lord...' [Ibrahim 14:1].
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is a succinct definition of the purpose (ghaya) of revelation. The author states that we can ascertain this purpose from the revealed texts themselves. He identifies two primary purposes. First, revelation serves as a universal message to all humanity: the Prophet is sent as a bearer of glad tidings (bashir) and a warner (nadhir), indicating that revelation brings both hope and caution. Second, revelation aims to bring people from the darknesses (zulumat) of ignorance, disbelief, and error into the light (nur) of guidance and truth, by God's permission. These two verses encapsulate the core function of revelation according to the author. The first is from Surat Saba', emphasizing the comprehensive nature of the Prophet's mission. The second is from Surat Ibrahim, framing revelation as a transformative light. This passage serves as the conclusion of the chapter's discussion on revelation.

البشارة والإنذار
الظلمات إلى النور

Key Takeaway
The purpose of revelation is twofold: to deliver glad tidings and warnings, and to guide humanity from darkness to light, as evidenced by the Quranic texts.

Characteristics of Revelation: Certainty, Innateness, and Objectivity
Author enumerates and explains key characteristics of revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the four key characteristics of revelation (yaqiniyya, lughat al-mithāl, wāqiʿiyya, fiṭriyya) as described in the passage, and connect them to Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Revelation is defined by certainty, objectivity, and innateness – not by human acquisition.

The author begins by listing four distinctive attributes of divine revelation that set it apart from human knowledge. These attributes – certainty, use of example language, objectivity, and innateness – establish the epistemic authority of revelation. We will unpack each attribute and examine the Quranic verses and usuli terminology that support them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the listed characteristics
Notice the author enumerates four characteristics with numbers: certainty and scientificity (al-yaqīniyya wa al-ʿilmiyya), language of example (lughat al-mithāl), objectivity (al-wāqiʿiyya), and innateness (al-fiṭriyya). Write them down.
The first characteristic pairs certainty with scientificity and stability.
2
Understand the explanation of each characteristic
Read the brief descriptions: for example, innateness is explained via Ibn Hazm's quote that God teaches prophets without learning. Objectivity means revelation deals with objective truths, not abstract ideas.
The Ibn Hazm quote is key to understanding fiṭriyya.
3
Connect to Quranic evidence
The author quotes several verses to support these characteristics. For instance, verses from Surah Maryam, Al-Hajj, etc., affirm that prophethood is by God's will alone. Note how each verse reinforces the idea of divine origin.
The verses collectively reject human acquisition of prophethood.
4
Summarize from the usuli perspective
The final paragraph rephrases these characteristics in technical usuli language: divine source, definitive transmission, definitive and conjectural indication, etc. Compare these with the initial list.
This shows how the same attributes are framed in legal theory.
